# PROLOGUE

*….it occurs to me that there is a proper balance between not asking enough of oneself and asking or expecting too much. *
*…. Not easy to find the balance, for if one does not have wild dreams of achievement, there is no spur…. *
***One must think like a hero to behave like a merely decent human being.***
- Sarton, May. Journal of a Solitude

This book is about being a merely decent investment professional.  Each section offers some perspective, and if you are open to it, some insight on our way of investing.   There are many ways to successfully invest – so the guidebook isn’t some sort of truth, or a general-purpose guide to investing in any fashion. It is specific to us.  All the ways to invest successfully are specific to the investor, and consistent over time.

Learning any specific skill means developing certain habits of mind.  This guidebook describes the habits of mind essential to our investment process.  Each of the three main parts of the book are describing the same habits from different perspectives:
- Notes & Letters: Descriptions of events as they happened and in describing events, they also describe how to look at the events and the habits of mind or principles involved.
- Principles & Processes: Short pieces discussing specific habits of mind or analytical frameworks intended as anchors for formal policies and procedures - anchoring corporate processes in certain habits of mind.
- Readings: Excerpts from different writers offering additional perspective or support on why we adopted different habits of mind.
The habits of mind described in the following pages are *tools for** “seeing” the present from a shared **perspective** and operating with shared processes*.  It may be that all we need to achieve excellence isn’t to have the “correct” perspective, but just that we have a shared perspective and shared processes.
Mastering the perspectives in the guidebook is not easy. It will require heroic efforts to develop the habits of mind described and apply them daily in our work.  And at the end of that heroic effort, we will only be doing decent or acceptable work***. ****** ******To paraphrase Sarton, one must think like a hero to ******do the work of****** a merely decent investment analyst.***

**
**

# PART 1

NOTES

# JANUARY 2022
## VALUE INVESTING, CHANGE & GORILLA GAMES

**Summary: The punchline is that the only opportunities we worth researching are what I am calling "value ****gorillas****" - these are large economic opportunities which are obvious problems with non-obvious / complex / hard solutions (****gorilla**** scale payoff to the winner****)**** but which are also invisible or ignored like the invisible ****gorilla**** in the famous attention experiment.  This "invisible" property tends to go along with low valuation or the expectations that the "****gorilla****" problem won't or can't be solved.**
### NUCLEAR AS AN INSTANCE OF A GENERAL CASE
The long-term opportunity in nuclear energy is obvious and has been for decades.  The regulatory issues and risks with nuclear have also been obvious.  And this is a key part of what's interesting and what makes for a large investment opportunity.  Big opportunities are NEVER esoteric or niche - they tend to be obvious, but hard and risky and complex in some ways.  Big opportunities end up being NEW COMBINATORIAL SOLUTIONS TO OLD KNOWN PROBLEMS.
New combinations of technologies or techniques to solve old problems may include small pieces which are new -- but usually the advantage comes from assembling technologies and techniques which are known to work in a new way.  It's not an invention, but a new combination of old inventions.  That's why the new combinatorial solution can scale as a business -- whereas a true invention will take time to learn about and learn how to operate.  This "combinatorial" idea is my own way of stating what folks from Joe Henrich the anthropologist states in his Secrets of Our Success to the process which science writer Amir Aczel describes in the Riddle of the Compass. The key insight one gets when you realize you are looking for scalable combinations of reliable tech, is that it means you can see these things coming in the potential unlocked by existing technologies.
Thus***, we have a model for understanding change by studying choke points or limiting steps in the value chains of different industries and asking experts or researching what potential existing technologies could be applied to those choke points.  Then we also look for people and businesses working on playing with or implementing those technologies, or who have intellectual capital in those areas which could become more valuable.***
***Nuclear energy is simply an instance of this general case.***  It sits at a point in the energy value chain.  Energy enables all industrial activity and frees people from labor allowing them to create and allocate their time to other activities, and the cost of energy is a key constraint in all economies so a technological implementation which could shift the cost of energy down is important.
Other instances of this general case include many instances of automation and virtualization, where the advanced manufacturing model using virtual design, a heterodox combination of constantly advancing manufacturing technologies including 3D printing as well other automated tools and robotics is "eating" legacy manufacturing.  This is the business Jabil and Flex are in.  Like the situation with GE & Westinghouse, these were also companies which had a large opportunity which was and still is emerging, but which traded at low valuations (single digit PE) on their existing/legacy businesses.
My original interest in crypto-currencies came from the simple fact that the charges from Visa and Mastercard or the "vig" on transactions in the US has gone up over time as technology has improved, whereas in other parts of the world like China - it has gone down.  This is a critical choke point that one can see with Visa/Mastercard as one of the largest expense items for folks like Home Depot behind lumber and labor.  And you could see people like Walmart and others working on finding ways around it.  Ultimately, with the payment systems - I think what is coming is that governments will offer different versions of CBDC's which function as public payment rails.  The CBDC becomes a publicly available open payments back-end which will open up competition in the payments space.  I know this is an explicit objective of the EU and Israel and China - and I believe it is part of the plan everywhere.  Not every choke point will have a private solution - but that's part of what makes it interesting.  In the case of payments and CBDC's -- these new networks will enable innovation on top of them.  That's what we will watch for.  Any bank with modern digital infrastructure will be able to plug into these systems - so the emergence of CBDC's will be an important event for banking generally.  We will finally see who has invested in their infrastructure and who hasn't -- and whoever hasn't will be quickly left behind. (Note: crypto is not a value gorilla - it is a gorilla large opportunity, but it is priced as though crypto has or will solve the problem - it's discounting success and not "invisible".)
Iraq is arguably also an instance of this where the new combinations are driven by the younger generation - so its demographics but specifically growing up digital.  The key lessons to learn from Iraq involve how developing economies onboard new consumers using technology.  Our hypothesis on the role of technology enabling emerging world consumers draws from Mark Miller's 2003 Digital Path essay.  Miller proposed that the internet could provide digital institutional infrastructure as an alternative to government and the traditional model. Miller's Digital Path is an alternative to Acemoglu's Narrow Path or DeSoto's Other Path.  The models of how we onboard new consumers through slowly evolving government institutions which are fundamentally rent seeking themselves won't work in the coming years if 3 billion consumers arrive from the developing world. We need to watch this.  The Digital Path is the only way, but technology is so abundant that the pervasive shifts between competition, conflict and cooperation between technology and governments is easy to forget.
Nuclear is one example of a general set of opportunities which involve:
1. The unlocking of a large amount of value through applying a combination of multiple old known technology to a choke point in a key value chain (Gorilla large, inspired by the old Geoffrey Moore book -https://www.harpercollins.com/products/the-gorilla-game-revised-edition-geoffrey-a-moore ).
2. Which is obvious, although not necessarily believable (Gorilla in the picture but did you notice it - http://theinvisiblegorilla.com/gorilla_experiment.html).
We can then look for companies which are working on these opportunities leveraging existing businesses and clients, and whose businesses trade as though the opportunities will not be realized (invisible gorilla / value stock / free real options).

### ALLOCATING OUR TIME: ONLY ONE GAME FOR US - VALUE GORILLA'S
Any framework is only as good as the use to which we put it.  In the case of this Value Gorilla Framework, the use is in targeting and sorting ideas as well as in checking the potential of current holdings.
John has discussed how the world is rapidly changing and when he is talking about that, where my mind always goes is to "what does that mean for what I do as an analyst?"   As an analyst the challenges are at least two-fold: (1) it's easy to get distracted by short-term events, which you don't want to ignore because they may create price volatility which is an opportunity to buy or sell at a better price - but which aren't worth more than a few percent at the margin long-term; (2) and then it is easy to get overwhelmed and fall back into looking at things that are smaller opportunities but feel easier to understand - and investing is not like baseball - in investing the home runs are more important than just getting on base.
As analysts -- in a world characterized by rapid change it becomes essential to focus on the large opportunities.  The pace of change means that you can ignore everything else.  Previously, the way I had articulated this idea was by saying that returns don't mean revert -- so this is another way of saying the old value investing model of buying cheap assets and waiting for mean reversion is dead.  We can argue about whether or not this model is really dead (it probably isn't) -- but the key idea is that there are better return opportunities in finding the companies creating new value than there is in reversion to mean valuations.  The creation of new value is a much bigger pie.  So that is what we need to focus on.
But we do not need to invest in opportunities which the market is already pricing as if they are a certainty.  We want the invisible gorillas.  And this is where the real work is.  The overall opportunities will be obvious - like the proverbial dollar bill on the street, such that the instinctive response will be "duh" or "but if it were going to happen it would have" - modern versions of if men were meant to fly they would have wings sort of thing.  So the work will be in understanding businesses which are working to solve these known problems.
This is where the idea of combinatorial innovation should be useful to us as analysts -- because we aren't looking for people really working on the bleeding edge, but on scaling known technologies and applying them in a new process. The new combinations may not be easy to understand, and they may be complex -- but the ideas won't be new or esoteric and the applications will be global.  This makes it a gorilla. The fact that the market is discounting a low probability of success makes it invisible.
***These are what we need to be focused on in a time of rapid change.  ***

### THE BASIC STORY OR **THESIS** FOR EACH **INVESTMENT** IS WHY ITS A VALUE GORILLA
The framework also works for how we communicate our ideas to each other and out investors.
Our elevator pitch of each investment should be why each holding is a value gorilla. What is the economic opportunity in front of it and what are they doing to exploit this opportunity? Why is it likely to succeed while the market is pricing the stock as if they will not succeed?  That's it.
Massmart is a value gorilla because of the scope of the retail opportunity over time in Africa, and the capabilities and ambitions of Walmart.  It's invisible now because of the poor performance of the business in the recent past, but new management and a fresh commitment from Walmart after the parent exited many other markets makes us think there is a will and a plan to succeed over time which isn't in the price of the stock.  Over the next 30 years, consumer growth in Africa should dwarf consumer growth in the rest of the world as the continent catches up economically and keeps growing in population long after populations in the rest of the world have stabilized or begun to fall.
Brookfield is a value gorilla because they have a disciplined global investment platform focused on refreshing the infrastructure assets of the developed world and building the new capacity that will be needed in the developing world.  Brookfield's investment capabilities are well known - but the magnitude of the long-term opportunity and the potential for extraordinary profits created by low-cost capital / low rates -- we believe this is what the market is underestimating.  Brookfield could compound at a 20% rate for a long-time, such that it is own sort of long-term compounding gorilla.  What is invisible in this case is the compound, or the old exponential vs linear case.

### CONCLUSION
Overall, I believe this simple framework offers us a unique and clear heuristic for allocating our time (managing ourselves as analysts) as well as prioritizing and sorting opportunities and communicating the thesis behind each idea within the team and with investors.
# JULY 2022
## WHAT JUST HAPPENED

*“**If you hold a cat by the tail, you will learn things you cannot learn any other way**”**.*
- Mark Twain

We launched the Merchant Adventure Fund in May of 2018 as an unconstrained global value oriented public equity portfolio.  Since then the world has surprised almost everyone with negative interest rates, negative oil prices, a global pandemic, the invention of new vaccines in record time, a land war in Europe supported by China, unprecedented developed world central bank and fiscal stimulus, followed now by rapid inflation, financial market enthusiasm for anything virtual and ethereal arguably leading to overinvestment only to be complemented with underinvestment in anything which could be touched – including oil, which after having a negative price two years ago now appears to be in short supply.  To cite a partial list.  The market has always been a harsh taskmaster. We were holding a cat by the tail and were learning things one couldn’t learn any other way.
Our mandate was to get out and learn about publicly traded companies around the word, looking for quality management teams and business franchises with significant growth opportunities and trading at low valuations.  We did this for a few years. Then the world closed.  We kept the same focus but worked virtually for the next two years.  In March 2022, a team member left the group. We took the opportunity to reflect on what we had learned through the pandemic, what had worked and what hadn’t.  The condensed list is below.

What worked:
- Constructing a long-term perspective based on domain expertise and financial analysis.
- Teaming up or gang-tackling the analysis of a company and industry.
- Being able to act on high conviction but non-consensus beliefs.
What didn’t:
1. Working Remotely… didn’t work.
2. Consensus Decision Making wasted a lot of energy but never achieved agreement.
3. Everything, everywhere, all at once – too many different businesses in too many places.

All organizations are learning and evolving.  By spending the last four years looking at “everything, everywhere all at once” we accelerated the learning curve for the team and have been able to define a focus and processes that we couldn’t have come to any other way.  Amar Bhide described this sort of trial-and-error process of building norms in a professional service firm in his classic 1995 paper on the evolution of McKinsey.  But I think Mark Twain captured the gist of it.  There are some things you can’t learn unless you hold a cat by the tail.  Going forward, we need to do less of what didn’t work and more of what worked.
This boils down to two principles:
- **Focus** on where we have an edge or can build an edge as an organization and in our investment process.
- **Imagine** how human agency and technological capabilities create future economic potential. **
**
## WHERE WE GO FROM HERE

*“…millennials and Gen-Z share values on a global scale**…**. For no previous generation could we so confidently pinpoint these or other common traits as we can for billions of young people today. The great divide in the world is therefore not East versus West or North versus South but young versus old.”**     -- **Parag Khanna, Move**.*

***We invest in human progress****** based on our understanding of human agency, technological capabilities, and economic potential.  Businesses uncover the economic potential and are the vehicles we invest in.  But businesses are driven by motivated individuals (human agency******)****** and they exploit scientific advances (technological capabilities).   This is a very simple and timeless model, but the implementation is complex and evolving.***
We know that the future hasn’t happened yet and that the most accurate way to model things that haven’t happened yet is in terms of probability trees. Many probabilities don’t fit normal distributions but are multi-modal - and sequences of events are path dependent.  The natural way for us to manage this complexity is to build stories or scenarios based on what we know – to imagine probable futures.  The value of this analysis is often not necessarily in predicting what path the future will take but in analytically preparing for multiple paths and having a list of events to watch for, which would represent important forks in the probability tree.
We must try to imagine the future - building scenarios based on (a) country demographics informed by (b) cultural anthropology and imagining major potential technological advances like (c) the digital path of development for emerging consumers or (d) nuclear fusion or (e) cellular rejuvenation.
The literature on scenarios goes in a number of different directions, but our general guides are Superforecasting by Tetlock and Using Scenarios by Chermak – both of which take a more function approach.  ***O******ur approach to scenarios and forecasting ******is described further in the Scenario Process in Part 2******.***

## ENDING WITH A NEW BEGINNING

*“All beginnings are hard.”*  -- Talmud
Business and investment is an adventure – there are neither guarantees nor certainty, only the adventure.  The first iteration of the fund challenged investors to hold diverse global risks in a single portfolio and trust our expertise on that diverse set of holdings.  While the thesis behind each of the positions may have been valid, what we learned was that it was impossible to (1) establish expertise on a diverse set of businesses and risks, and (2) maintain a unified team perspective while staying abreast of  “everything everywhere all at once.”
During the first iteration of the fund, we added team members focused on different geographies and sectors, added junior analysts and interns as well as domain experts in software and biotech.  We added knowledge and expertise as the team grew, but we never reconciled divergent perspectives of experienced analysts. The largest gap in perspectives always surfaced in what the key drivers of change were -- rather than any disagreement about current facts.  Based on the problem of unifying analyst perspectives – we need a unified set of key drivers of change.
The farm team concept also failed for similar reasons.  In the process of taking small farm team positions which could be advocated by a single analyst, we ended up with holdings based on divergent perspectives which couldn’t be reconciled.  Small “farm” holdings ended up being a distraction and creating conflicts more than they created learning or opportunities.  The hypothesis behind having a farm team was as a learning mechanism – but this didn’t pan out.  There are better ways to build learning into the culture.
Our best work involved teams of a (1) junior analyst who brought a younger perspective and could also help with blocking and tackling aspects of the analysis, (2) a domain expert with decades of knowledge and experience in the field the subject company was focused on, (3) a senior analyst to integrate the domain expertise with the young optimism and energy and a financial model and overall investment thesis.  Building cross functional teams and working more in these teams is one of the best ways to drive organizational performance, unify perspectives, build culture and encourage learning.

Our experiences investing in Japan, Russia and China also taught us a diverse set of lessons which boiled down to one thing: societies whose populations aren’t growing will face difficulties.  It is obvious – young people are the future. If societies have low birth rates and emigration, then those societies are failing and shrinking.  Our experience investing in what looked like low valuation assets that just tended to get cheaper and cheaper in shrinking states led us to step back and consider what to do differently.

***Remembering that investing is about the future and that it works best when we are investing in human progress, we chose to focus our attention on countries or markets where:***
- ***Large enough domestic market to have some scale (40 million or more in population).***
- ***Not shrinking (population hasn’t declined over the last 5 years). ***
- ***Connected to the global economy (at least one ******mega city******).  ***
***We decided to focus on these demographically well positioned countries – but it is important to note that we are choosing an area to focus on rather than creating negative screens or limits saying we won’t invest in other places.  Rather than constructing limits, we are quantifying and enumerating preferences or advantages. ***
Additionally, to remain a research-driven organization we needed to accept that administrative costs limit where we invest.  *C**ountry funds are the appropriate way for investors to put money to work in attractive geographies which have limited capital markets and/or administrative and capital constraints – part of focusing the fund is to focus on open capital markets w**ith** lower administrative costs and complexity*.

***The new beginning for the fund is the simple process of putting the lessons of our first four years to work:  ***
- ***Focusing on areas of structural change in the global economy.  ***
- ***Focusing on economically and financially functional geographies. ***
- ***Focusing on fewer companies.  ***
- ***Focusing on building knowledge of these opportunities in teams.  ***
***… Focus, focus, focus.***

## WHEN ON THE BATTLEFIELD

*“I often say that when you can measure what you are speaking about, and express it in numbers, you know something about it: but when you cannot measure it, when you cannot express it in numbers, your knowledge is of a meagre and unsatisfactory kind…”**	*
*	**	**	**	**	-- William Thomson (Lord Kelvin) *

***Gerald Loeb famously wrote about the battle for investment survival during the great depression.  Most companies are losing investments over time, not winners.  The market tends to ******appreciate****** because the winners create more wealth than the losers destroy over time.  To survive and prosper on the investment battlefield one needs to identify these winners – but the market is perverse, and profits come from the gap in the expectations in the purchase price of an asset versus future expectations.  Profits over time come from rising expectations.  So, our task is ******identifying****** the winners who will drive the markets in the future, but at a time when they aren’t expected to win.***

Our task in the battle for investment survival is *quantifying* the gap between the expectations in current company prices and our expectations for what a company can achieve given the economic potential, technological capabilities, and human talent it has and is targeting.  While quantifying expectations in a current stock price is a pedestrian task, imagining the future paths of a business and putting numbers to it is the furthest thing from pedestrian and involves a high degree of creativity, persistence, and humility.
Since investing is all about the future – the starting point for our analysis is to construct and maintain a perspective on where the world is going in the long-term.  This starts with the economic geography of the world, and then adds an analysis of key drivers of change in the world.   The maps and models and frameworks are all about being able to express in nodes and edges (graphs and numbers) the current state and economic context of companies.  Measuring their capabilities and potential.  We build our map, invest based on it.  ***It’s very simple at a conceptual level – the devil is in the details of how we collect the information, process it as a team and implement what we learn in the portfolio.***

## THE LOOP:  Economic Potential, Technological Capabilities & Human Agency

With any framework, its value is in its use.  The framework’s value isn’t in being “true” – but in providing a shared language and terms for the implementation and coordination of a research team for the purpose of building a global equity portfolio.
Operationally, the research and portfolio management process is a battlefield decision-making loop which starts with identifying where we will fight for profits (areas of economic potential – products, industries and geographies), who and what are exceptional in those areas (technological capabilities and human agency – companies and managements), then observing and calibrating (doing the work to gather the information, test assumptions, learn and adapt).   The devil is in the details of each step – and in each section below we discuss what work products are involved in each step, with an eye toward the tasks ahead of us as we build our organizational capabilities.

### ECONOMIC POTENTIAL

*“Long-term trends can be predicted, although their specifics cannot.”*   -- Kevin Kelly
We break long-term trends and structural changes into three categories or domains: biological, physical, and digital.  Since we are investing based on where the world is going, we think of diversification in the portfolio in terms of forces driving change in these three domains of the economy.  These different domains each map to multiple sectors in the economy as shown in the table below.

|  | Biological | Physical | Digital |
| --- | --- | --- | --- |
|  | Biotech & Health Technology | Advanced Materials & Manufacturing | Distributed compute and networks |
|  |
| Communications |  |  | **+** | **+** |
| Consumer Discretionary |  | **+** | **+** | **+** |
| Consumer     Staples |  | **+** | **+** |  |
| Energy |  | **+** | **+** | **+** |
| Financials |  |  |  | **+** |
| Health Care |  | **+** | **+** | **+** |
| Industrials |  |  | **+** | **+** |
| Materials |  | **+** | **+** |  |
| Real Estate |  | **+** | **+** | **+** |
| Technology |  | **+** | **+** | **+** |
| Utilities |  |  | **+** | **+** |
|  |

***Our perspectives on change in the biological, physical, and digital worlds will be captured in a series of****** scenarios following the process described in Part 2******.  ***
In assessing economic potential, in addition to broad economic trends, we need to pay attention to changes in human geography – which is basically where people are today versus where they are expected to be tomorrow.  Human geography in this context is best expressed, as Ai Weiwei put it, as human flow.  While this is complicated, if we take a few basic criteria – the world’s human geography comes into a clear focus.
***Only seven countries have open capital markets, at least ******one mega city******, a domestic population above 40 million, and a population which is growing over the last five years: ***
**Brazil, Indonesia, Mexico, South Africa, Turkey, United Kingdom, United States.**
*In terms of economic potential, our focus is on businesses positioned to benefit from innovation in those growing geographies which are also open to foreign capital.****  ***
***The countries below meet all the criteria above, except they have certain capital market or government restrictions that cause us to exclude them from our open markets list:***
***Argentina, Bangladesh, Democratic Republic of Congo, Egypt, Ethiopia,***
***India, Iran, Iraq, Kenya, Nigeria, Pakistan, Philippines, Vietnam******.***

*Note: A discussion of the country framework is included in the Principles and Process section on the Composition of Economic Growth.*

### TECHNOLOGICAL CAPABILITY & HUMAN AGENCY

*“The future is already here, it’s just not evenly distributed.”  -- William Gibson*

Businesses make money by mobilizing technological capabilities and human agency – and in the process of doing this they take something that was hard and make it easier, lowering transaction costs and driving human progress.
Businesses don’t do well with fundamental discovery and long-term research.  What businesses do well is scale and breadth – to commodify & distribute innovations and discoveries.  Businesses are the mechanism for distributing the future. And in distributing the future, they often create wealth.
The purpose of our company research process is to identify exceptional businesses which are characterized by some combination of unique and leading technical capabilities and people – distributing the future and driving human progress.  Our company research framework is outlined below.
1. Business Franchise
How does the business take one dollar and turn it into two?
What are the technical skills and advantages the company has?
Nuts and bolts of the financial model and scaling.

2. Management Quality / Culture
Leadership & culture are key to re-investment of capital & organizational resilience.
There are no exceptional results without exceptional people doing exceptional work.

3. Valuation:
Gap between business potential and expectations discounted in current price.
Napkin math captured in versions of a Gordon Growth Model:
*Value = Profits / (Target Return – Growth Rate)*
*Value (Price / Sales) = Normal Net Margin / (Target Return – Sales Growth Rate)*
*Value (Price / Book) = Normal ROE / (Target Return – Book Growth Rate)*

Perspectives on business franchise, management quality, and valuation combine to form our investment thesis on each company in the portfolio.  Technical capabilities influence the business franchise. Management quality and corporate culture are all about human agency – motivating, engaging, growing, compounding and retaining human intellectual capital.  Valuation is the step of identifying the gap between our expectation and the expectations in the current price, to maximize the probability of profiting from rising expectations (or multiple expansion).  ***This information will all be captured in the Investment Thesis Notes for each company in ******Sentieo******.***
### A NOTE ON VALUATION
We are not trying to create complex models for the valuation thesis, but to imagine a business at scale operating in a steady state.  The purpose of the valuation framework is to get a target price to sales or price to book multiple.
From a nuts-and-bolts perspective, our target return is 15% and the maximum long-term sales or book growth rate for the purpose of deriving a target valuation multiple is 10%, thus, for any subscale business with long-term growth potential the target multiple to sales = Normal Net Margin / 0.05; or target multiple to book value = Normal ROE / 0.05.  Different businesses are best characterized as being driven either by invested capital producing a return (book value multiple, ROE & book growth framework) or by cranking up sales (sales multiple, net margin, sales growth framework).  Part of knowing a business is knowing which is a better characterization.
Once we really know a business, figuring out the target multiple and our target price becomes the easy part.  This is an important characteristic of a useful valuation discipline – it focuses one on understanding businesses and offers a quick and easy way to compare opportunities and doesn’t get bogged down in technical details of modelling.  Implementations of technically precise models all get precision at the price of understanding the way a business is evolving and being able to compare that precise model to other companies.  This is like Heisenberg’s uncertainty principle applied to investments: you can know exactly what a business is now or where it’s going, but not both.  Investing is about the future, not the past – so we want to know where the businesses are going.
### OBSERVATION & CALIBRATION
Scenario planning and investing are both focused on possible and probable futures, so it makes sense that Peter Schwartz’s description of scenario planning also applies to investing:
*“… planning involves imagining different possibilities and then how we can get from here to there.  And **so** it requires a combination of two things: imagination and analytical realism.”*

Most investment operations do analytical realism very well but building a culture that supports imagining an uncertain future and positing scenarios which will certainly be wrong in many ways – well, very few firms do that well.  ***Our goal is to build a culture which both supports a disciplined analytical process (realism) as well as cultivating a long-term view drawn from imagined path******s****** from here to the future.***
All company observations are tested against the investment thesis.  The investment thesis includes a set of factors to watch for or observe to confirm or refute the investment thesis.  Calibration is the modification of the investment thesis and the decisions to buy and sell based on the thesis or changes in the thesis.  All that matters is exactly what we buy and sell, when we do it and where the expectations are in the price at the time of purchase or sale.  All the steps, frameworks, principles, and processes leading to this point are like the gears that deliver force from the engine (the economy) to the wheels (the portfolio).  Buys and sells are where the rubber meets the road.
The best description of what it is to be an observer is captured in Peter Drucker’s autobiography The Adventures of a Bystander.  As observers, we ask questions and listen to the response.  Observing is a mix of the art of being curious and asking the right questions and being an active listener – hearing and seeing what is there, and not just watching for what one expects.
Our portfolio construction guidelines describe when we buy or sell shares, and which companies we consider buying or selling shares in.  The portfolio construction guidelines are also subject to observation, calibration, and change.
The changes in the portfolio construction guidelines from the first iteration of the fund to now include:
- Private company investments as described in Part 2,
- Focusing on companies with smaller capitalizations, limited to less than USD $25 billion,
- Diversifying the portfolio across drivers of economic change rather than economic sectors,
- Focusing on fewer companies (30-40 holdings) and eliminating the farm team of small holdings, and
- Owning larger positions of each of the fewer in the portfolio.
The purpose of all these adjustments were to incorporate what we learned from “holding the market” by the tail.  The portfolio construction guidelines are listed below.
*Rules:  *
  - *Minimum 70% Public Equities / Maximum 10% Cash, *
  - *Room in between for select Private Company investments, or Convertible debt,*
  - *Maximum Market Capitalization of $25 Billion.*

*Diversification:*
  - *Portfolio diversification is viewed in terms of the different themes or areas of structural economic change. The portfolio is invested across this set of structural changes which the manager **view** as significant but does not seek to be broadly economically diversified in any traditional sense.*
  - *The portfolio is concentrated, holding **2**0 to 40 positions. *

*Position Sizing – Buy/Sell Discipline: *
  - *Research** process constantly calibrates, questions, and refines each company investment thesis.*
  - *The investment thesis focuses on business franchise, management quality and low valuation.*
  - *If we are confident of 1 of the 3 factors, the position is 1%, 2 of 3 is 2%, 3 of 3 is 3%.*
  - *Weights over 3% reflect confidence in all 3 factors plus something exceptional. *
  - *When our assessment changes significantly the position size is adjusted.*

## CLOSING: COMING BACK TOGETHER AS A TEAM
We do our best work as a team.  The greatest cost of the covid epidemic for us as an organization is that “sheltering in place’ robbed us of the experience of being in the “foxhole” together during the epidemic.  We didn’t come together as a team because we just weren’t together very much.
At this point, the fund has largely transitioned to this new approach.  We have 36 holdings in the portfolio excluding the Euphrates Iraq Fund, down from a pandemic high of 85 names at one point.  All 36 of those holdings are in the way of biotech or health technology, advanced materials and manufacturing or distributed compute and networking.  None of the companies are over $25 billion in market cap.  The Euphrates Fund interest will be redeemed effective 9/30 with the cash returned to the fund around mid-October.
Operationally, the key steps between now and mid-October are to implement the new process and re-introduce the fund.  But organizationally, we need to make sure we come together as a team and that requires that members of the portfolio management team be in the same place working together as much as possible, and that we adhere to a disciplined investment meeting schedule for the fund.
Specifically, we will review the portfolio each Thursday afternoon and make any changes to the guidelines in the portfolio in the weekly meeting.  Outside of the weekly meeting, portfolio guidelines will not be changed.  Trading toward portfolio guidelines may happen throughout the week based on written instructions/recommendations/targets from the portfolio management team and included in the notes from the weekly team meeting.  This process will be detailed in an Observable or a Sentieo notebook (tbd) and the portfolio guidelines spreadsheet and notes will be kept in a format linked to the process notebook.
All of these lessons, principles and processes could be perfectly correct – but none of that will matter without our agency, without us coming together as a team with shared principles and a common set of tools and processes so that our efforts magnify each other rather than competing with each other.  This magnification is difficult to achieve – but at its root I think Ed Catmull from Pixar captured it best in a practice he called “plus-ing” which can be summarized by saying that you have to say both what you like and don’t like about something if you are going to say anything at all.  If all you have to say is no, then you don’t have anything to say.  And if all you have to say is “yes” – well you might not have been paying attention.  A quality dialog is driven by detailed observations – good and bad both, and then listening and adaptation or calibration.

# Excerpts from past Fund Letters
## JUNE 2018

*And this was one of the wonders of the new company: ****the adventurers knew so little about the world****. They believed they had some geographical knowledge; they had their investors’ money; they had confidence. And they took such a big step, feeling **as** they went the shape of a world known only from maps and the pronouncements of the cosmographical authorities. Within a single reflex were contained both formidable ambition and startling ignorance. *

*In a calculating, pragmatic and essentially conservative world, it was a bold and imaginative leap into the unknown. And it would have huge consequences for England. The ancestry of what later became Britain’s mature mercantile empire needs to be traced back to this root – a single, ambitious and (from one perspective at least) hare-brained project that grew up in and around London in the early 1550s. *

***Alford, Stephen*****. ****London's Triumph: Merchants, Adventurers, and Money in Shakespeare's City**** **

### CLEAR COMPASS
The Merchant Adventure Fund is designed to get in the way of the three areas listed below, and other, “misunderstood and mispriced” phenomena.  The first point focuses on corporations as the relevant entities to understand and invest in, avoiding the circular and cyclical asset allocation madness many go through.  The second point asks whether the way we live in the West reflects a culture or whether the way we live is just how humans live when they have a certain level of health care and material wealth. The Gapminder Foundation would call this living on income Level 4, and assert that our income levels define us more than other factors.  On the third point, the network connects and coordinates the agents in the economy.
The three key systematic factors are:
- *** Creation of wealth through corporations***.  I am ceaselessly amazed that investors don’t get this.  Around the world, the corporation is the vehicle for wealth creation. But it gets the same respect Churchill gave democracy as the least bad form of government.

- ***Expansion of Basic Health Care***, including maternal and infant care.  The average number of births per woman fell from about 5.6 in 1966 (close to where it had been for all human history) to a level just over 2 where it sits today.  The median family in the world today is 4 people everywhere but in parts of Africa.  First time ever.  The flip side of births is life expectancy.  As life expectancy increased, births fell.  And falling births are a precondition for women’s rights and equality movements. The significance of the global public health efforts which catalyzed this change cannot be overstated.

- ***Exponential expansion of networks***.  We thought the late 1990’s internet was big – but that was just wiring a few hundred million fixed machines together.  What’s happening now involves over a billion smart phones, data centers full of servers, a network upgrade to 5G and new generations of autonomous devices.  We haven’t even started yet.  This connectivity is more like the development of a new sensory function than anything else, like eyes during the pre-Cambrian explosion.
***In these three points, we have a powerful model of how a single dominant and stable household structure for consumption and a single dominant and stable wealth creation structure have both emerged***. This sort of “standard consumption and production model” hasn’t ever existed before on a global scale – and it only emerged in the last few decades enabled by advances in global health. With the network connectivity, we have a substrate that connects and coordinates these consumption and production entities.  This is what our society has evolved into, and once we discern that reality – we can start to ask where this all leads and how to profit and prosper in a future which will be fundamentally new in many ways.  ***
***
## SEPTEMBER 2018

A good opening sentence, like a good investment theme isn’t a summary – it’s the entrance. The Merchant Adventure Fund itself is built on a few core themes.  They are our assumptions, or compass. Many market participants don’t agree with our assumptions, so it is worth explaining and exploring them.  The first assumption we make is that:
**The best way to profit over time is through the ownership of publicly traded corporations that are in the way of economic growth. **
This simple sentence is an entrance to its own story.  The story starts with the creation of corporations as a way for people to coordinate and combine labor and capital to buy resources for a dollar and sell products for two or more.  Few people focus on the corporation in this way – as the opening sentence of the story of global economic growth.
Ownership, or “title” to assets or corporations is the instrument used to mobilize capital.  In Elizabethan England, the role of ownership wasn’t only critical to the colonies which were created by companies of merchant adventurers – it was also critical to the publication of the First Folio and Shakespeare’s immortality.  As Emma Smith documents in *The Making of Shakespeare’s First Folio*, the key to the publication of the folio in the years after Shakespeare’s death was the fact that the Kings Men held title to the plays and the aging players, Heminge and Condell, hatched a monetization strategy.  Understanding the importance or ownership leads one to look for clean title and adopt a long-term perspective.

…. A key part of this is the role of **price**, or *the expectations discounted in the price* at which the public markets trade ownership interests in businesses.  Can we become part owners for a price that reflects lower expectations for the business than it is likely to achieve?  In other words, is it a low valuation company with good long-term prospects?  The expectations in the price of a stock are like height of the hurdles in a race, and the company is the runner.  We like to invest in businesses with the hurdling ability of Edwin Moses, but where the market’s price sets the hurdles at the height of a matchbox.
***… ******Our goal in building the business is to be small but fierce.  To punch above our weight.  We won’t accomplish this by being smarter or dressed better than others.  We will do it by working more efficiently and focusing on a different set of key factors and drivers.  Our assumptions are different and the differentiator.  As any logician knows, different assumptions necessarily lead to different conclusions.***
… We are focused on what will be next. Specifically, we believe that the falling cost of sequencing and manipulating genetic information may be crossing a point after which it will rapidly change the way we all live.  Owning a “bundle of biotechs” isn’t a normal position for a value portfolio to carefully research, build and maintain.  But investment value isn’t based on past performance or past business results.  Investment value is driven by the difference between the expectations in the market price and the future performance of a company.
**… ****All values are values in use, and businesses continually change and evolve.**  Our focus is on understanding businesses as they are now and as they will be.  Effort spent explaining what was, while trying to manage a portfolio based on what will create value is a misallocation of scarce intellectual capital and a “bad” anchor – and a disadvantage.   We pay close attention to the anchors we set for ourselves and others through our activities.  When we look at the task before us – stubbornly avoiding accidental anchors in the analysis of companies and economies is a key advantage we have.
## DECEMBER 2018

*“It is a world of change in which we live, and a world of uncertainty. We live only by knowing something about the future; while the problems of life, or of conduct at least, arise from the fact that we know so little. This is as true of business as of other spheres of activity. The essence of the situation is action according to opinion, of greater or less foundation and value, neither entire ignorance nor complete and perfect information, but partial knowledge.”*
*Knight, Frank **H..** **Risk, Uncertainty, and Profit*

### UNCERTAINTY & PROFIT

Given the recent market volatility and chatter about being late in the cycle or early in a trade war, we thought it might be useful to go back to the basics, which means going back to Frank Knight’s classic book Risk, Uncertainty & Profit.  Knight asserts that profit in organizations comes from confronting uncertainty with partial knowledge.
We follow Knight’s advice and look for profit in areas of uncertainty – in the elements which evade any reliable quantification and where we must rely on partial information, opinion, and conjecture.  We believe our fascination with uncertainty gives us a key competitive advantage as investors.  Most analysts and investors are either trained to avoid structural uncertainty, or they find uncertainty too hard to deal with because of organizational pressures to be “right.”
When wading into areas of economic uncertainty, we will make mistakes. The only question is how wrong we will be.  Will we make big mistakes or small mistakes?  Being wrong bothers us, but it bothers us less than missing big opportunities for profit.  We try to make a lot of “small” mistakes in the pursuit of “big” opportunities.  Profit comes from uncertainty, and confronting uncertainty comes with a high error rate.
But what does this mean? How does one confront an uncertain future and manage through it? This is best captured by a simple picture from a research report written in the time before spreadsheets.  The drawing shows the investors as stick figures stuck in the present: faces up against a wall, trying to look into the future.  The lone investor at the bottom has a different perspective, separate from the others and focused on future profits.  That is what we are trying to do (below).
*from a 1980’s Research Report by John Gunn*

## MARCH 2019

*Models create structures within which we can think logically…. But the logic comes at a cost… *
*All models are wrong…. They omit details. *
*          Page, Scott **E..** **The Model Thinker: What You Need to Know to Make Data Work for You** *
*          (p. 6).  Basic Books. Kindle Edition.*

F. Scott Fitzgerald noted “the test of a first-rate intelligence is the ability to hold two opposed ideas in mind at the same time and still retain the ability to function.”  The two opposed ideas we grapple with are captured in Page’s quote above (which is a reprise of George Box’s original observation):  models give us the structure to think but, are also wrong because they simplify.
This battle between simplicity and complexity has been famously mapped onto hedgehogs and foxes: hedgehogs see & seek simplicity; foxes see & seek complexity.  Jim Collins popularized the metaphor in Good to Great and Phil Tetlock also utilized it in his book Superforecasting.  The tension between seeing the layers of complexity like a fox, and the need to use simplified models to analyze and communicate information is also the fundamental challenge of investment research.
We use accounting to summarize sets of transactions, but in building the accounts in financial statements – information is omitted.  Some of these omissions are filled in by financial footnotes – but much of the work of investment research is going from the simplified accounting model in the financial statements back to the complexity of the underlying business, without ever being able to see all the complexity because of a host of constraints.  A first-rate investment research process has to pass the same test Fitzgerald poses for a “first rate intelligence” – holding opposing ideas in the mind at the same time and maintaining the ability to buy, sell or wait.
Some have suggested to us that the idea of being a value investor and a biotech investor at the same time is a specific example of opposed ideas, which we hold at the same time.  We would like to take a little time now to explain the reasoned basis for this position as well as the basic theses behind the different biotech holdings in the fund.
To begin with, we look for a basic set of factors which genuinely aren’t unique: strong balance sheets, quality management and consequential target markets.  But then we add our own perspective.  In this context as well as others, our own perspective is often about being patient when others aren’t – when we are dealing with a business where the basic factors of quality management, cash and consequential targets markets all check out.
For instance, if a quality management team has been working on a specific set of problems or pathways with limited success for decades and the market has become disillusioned such that the stock trades at a discount to the amount of capital which has been invested in research over time – well that interests us.  We take a used car approach to buying what we believe are quality management teams when their stocks are trading at a discount to what they have spent in research.  We are always attempting to buy research in process at a discount.
Each of the standard factors which we look for serves a practical purpose:
*strong balance sheet* - having cash on the balance sheet to buy time and allow for the complexity and challenges that are part of any genuine research process;
*quality management* - the resilience of a management team to keep pushing for the goal or intelligently pivot;
*consequential target markets* - the relevance and economic importance of the treatment or technology if the company does achieve its goals.
**
**
## JUNE 2019

### THE HUSTLE

*Someone's always buying, someone's always selling, and the hustle is about trying to be in the middle of that whole thing. None of it was legal. Nobody knew where anything came from. The guy who got us Nikes, did he really have a "staff discount?" You don't know. You don't ask. It's just, "Hey, look what I found" and "Cool, how much do you want?"*
*          Trevor Noah, **Born a Crime**.*

Trevor Noah captured the insanity of bad governance and its’ consequences in his autobiography Born a Crime.  Bad or inadequate governance forces people to get what they need to survive through what we call “informal” means.  Noah called it “the hustle” – and that captures it almost perfectly. Everyone is just trying to hustle to stay alive or get what they can to pull themselves up just a little bit.  The important distinction we need to make from Noah’s description of “the hustle” is that in the way we think of it, “hustling” includes both legal and illegal activities in the informal economy.
As long as you are caught in *the hustle*, you can’t get far because as Hernando de Soto noted -- you don’t own what you own.  *The hustle* isn’t about Confucian or Protestant values – you don’t get into or out of *the hustle* because of your beliefs.  Beliefs have nothing to do with it.  *The hustle* is the natural human state in the absence of functioning sets of rules and institutions.
The study of how humanity escapes *the hustle* is often referred to as global development.  One of the best resources on global development is the Gapminder Foundation (gapminder.org).  Gapminder breaks the world into four income levels and shows pictures on their Dollar Street website (dollarstreet.org) of what life looks like in different countries at the different income levels.  Each “figure” in the graphic below represents one billion people, and the graphic illustrates roughly how many people in the world today live at different income levels.

Level 1 is “extreme poverty” – people living on less than $2 per day.  In the early 1970’s, one out of two people in the world lived in extreme poverty.  Half of the global population of humans was malnourished and miserable.  Now that number is down to roughly one in ten.
The shift of the global population out of extreme poverty, beginning to escape *the hustle*, is extraordinary.  Forty percent of the population of the world is moving up to higher income levels and hopefully on their way to joining the global consumer economy as a result. In the graphic, the upwardly mobile forty percent is represented by the 3 billion people currently in Income Level 2 – or people living on between 2 to 8 USD per day.  To offer a more complete picture of global development, Gapminder has created their “map of the world” – shown below with 2015 data:

The map offers another useful view of the data.  The details of development in each country are messy and confusing – but the near universal fact is that most of the world is on the march toward longer life spans and greater wealth.  Whenever one hears talk of low interest rates and secular stagnation, it is useful to remember the facts about global development and that 40% of the global population is on the way to the global consumer party but haven’t arrived yet.  Those 3 billion or so people will be arriving in the next two decades.  Fewer than 3 billion people are in the consumer economy today (those in Level 3 and Level 4), so the number of people at this “party” will more than double in the next twenty years.  Those 3 billion people coming to the consumer party have all already been born.  This isn’t theoretical.
So how will these people escape *the hustle *and be able to build material wealth?  The specifics will differ for each person and in each country – but building wealth will generally require accounts of some sort.  Extending the financial system by banking the unbanked (or under-banked) continues to be one of the largest business opportunities in the world.

To repeat ourselves (which we do often) we think the recent market gyrations confirm the enduring wisdom of Josef de La Vega’s from 1688: “*Whoever wishes to win in this game must have patience and money, since the values are so little **constant** and rumors so little founded on truth.”  *What would have meaning for us would be a shift or shuffling in valuation gaps in the market.  Dramatic moves in the market in the last few quarters have not been accompanied by any significant “shuffling” of the deck or shifts in valuations.  It has been as if a dealer threw a deck of cards into the air and they landed back on the table in the same order they were in before, or as if the dealer kept dealing the same cards unshuffled.  The game will change if the market (the dealer) starts shuffling the deck.

CLOSING
In their the June 2019 letter, the managers of the Euphrates Iraq Fund observed that:
*Our recent trip made it plain that everyone from the Iraqi entrepreneur to the C-suite executive in downtown Baghdad to the average guy taking Uber to work are driven not by the headlines of war and misery that distort our understanding of their country, but by the actual conditions in the world in which they live. Those conditions are far from ideal, but they are also much improved from the conditions that prevailed five years ago. That very real improvement is responsible for the growing sense of optimism we saw in Baghdad.*
When one gets out into the world and looks – people everywhere are working to improve their lives and build something for their families.  In Iraq, Turkey, as well as the US and the UK and in the banks and most other holdings in the fund -- we get to invest in the success of people and businesses at multiples of earnings which are lower than the sales multiples of most of the supposedly safe investments in US technology, payments, media or consumer stocks.
We believe that in time, the investments at low multiples in businesses or geographies currently perceived as high risk will be safer than all the money many feel safe investing at high multiples in business franchises and places that they believe are safe.  Time will tell.  But as de la Vega observed, the only certainty is that values will be little constant and the rumors little founded on truth.  Given these facts of the market, we suggest anchor yourself against the inconstancy of the market by getting out in the world and looking around -- the billions of people hustling to build material wealth are hard to miss, and amazing to see at work.

## SEPTEMBER 2019

*Shakespeare was a master of **ellipsis**, of leaving things out**..**.*
Bloom, Harold. Cleopatra: I Am Fire and Air

In How to Read and Why, Harold Bloom exhorts us to “read deeply, not to believe, not to accept, not to contradict, but to learn.”  Reading closely or “deeply” is much like listening -- it is about hearing both what is said and what is not said, as well as how it is said.  In other of his books, Bloom repeatedly highlights one Shakespeare’s skills as knowing what to leave out or unsaid – the “master of ellipsis” was how he would express it.
Many will wonder what in the world a literary critic has to do with investing and business analysis – but I would point out two facts (1) much of business is just drama acted off-stage, and (2) most of a business or security analyst’s time is spent reading or interviewing professionals about their businesses.  As business analysts, we are business critics – the domain is business, but large parts of the skillset involved in performing criticism are the same as what’s used in literature, drama or any other field.  Much of what we do as investment analysts is reading, so the question of how to read well and actively is essential.  Just as one must interpret Shakespeare – one must interpret and be able to fill in the ellipses and gaps left in the narratives of CEO’s annual letters or business reports in the Financial Times or Wall Street Journal.  Our job is to read and listen with a critical eye and ear -- to mind the gaps in what is said as well as the gaps in our own understanding.
The point of criticism in any domain is not just to criticize – but to see clearly.  In The Anatomy of Influence, Bloom cites one of his mentors teaching him that the heart of criticism is appreciation.  The opposite of appreciation and criticism is mediocrity.  The mediocre eye sees what it is told to see and the mediocre ear hears what it is told to hear.  The mediocre reader catches the headline and whatever points reinforce his or her existing beliefs and learns nothing.  In investing, systematic effects or factors and mediocrity are synonymous.  Excellence and learning are idiosyncratic.  Our fund portfolio is idiosyncratic as well.  Time will tell if the fund achieves excellence, but that is our unrelenting goal.  Although he has passed away, Bloom left clear thoughts on mediocrity:
*… we certainly owe mediocrity nothing, whatever collectivity **it** *
*purports to advance or at least represent.* (How to Read and Why).
Collective forms of mediocrity dominate the current business environment, from index funds to negative interest rates.  We believe that one should be careful of these institutionalized forms of mediocrity.  Blindly relying on them for your future wealth and investment returns might not work as well as the crowds suggest.

### A COMMENT ON NEGATIVE RATES & OWNER OPERATORS
It is important to understand what negative rates mean.  The market clearing interest rate is the return on capital in an economy for activity at different time horizons.  Negative interest rates mean “investing” capital at negative returns -- at a loss.  Negative rates tell us that a lot of businesses in an economy should be out of business, and that the default, bankruptcy or capital recycling mechanisms in an economy are broken.  In a business context, negative rates are a quantitative indicator of economies which have acquiesced to mediocrity.
We continue to the believe that one wants to own the equity of companies that produce a positive return on capital.  Specifically, within the universe of companies which are building economic value, we like owning shares in businesses where management is also a large shareholder.  We refer to these businesses as owner-operators.
We do not have a precise quantitative definition for owner operators.  We apply a threshold test for the overlap of ownership of the shares and operational control or influence.  The test resembles Supreme Court Justice Potter’s famous “I know it when I see it” test for obscenity.  As of the end of the 3rd quarter, approximately 38% of the fund portfolio was invested in 25 different companies we categorize as owner-operators.  A quick scan of the list’s sectors and countries of the companies shows that when we discuss owner-operators we aren’t talking about any specific industry or country.  What makes owner-operators special isn’t the specific business or its location, but the higher probability of alignment of management interests and efforts with our own long-term goals as shareholders.

As stated before: our reason for investing in a business, or our ***investment hypothesis***, is made up of four general parts: ***business franchise, growth opportunities, ******management****** and valuation***.  We call it a hypothesis instead of a thesis because we are always learning and never entirely correct.  In investing, one makes educated guesses and takes profits or losses. The investment hypothesis is a structure for making educated guesses about the future value of a business.

CLOSING
If one takes Mr. Bloom’s advice on criticism and appreciation to heart, and rejects the rolling tide of mediocrity – a close reading of current events offers some clues of where the world may be going and how we might profit:
- WeWork may be a sign that multiple expansion in one part of the markets, driven by a single large investor, is spent.  If WeWork signals the end of the willingness of investors to ‘suspend disbelief” and the use of mathematics in valuing large private companies then this could be the beginning of a return to sanity in private markets, where valuations have been above public market valuations for quite a while now.
- Approximately 45% of the S&P 500 trades above 4x price-to-sales (P/S) at the end of September.  Since the S&P 500 is made up of companies that are already large, the idea that well over a third of these large companies are going to be able to maintain profitability and growth at high levels perpetually into the future is very, very, very improbable.  Large, quickly growing, highly profitable companies tend to attract competition, government scrutiny or both.
- After the most recent ECB rate cut, the “knives” come out for Mr. Draghi before he has even left the building.  The open criticism of the failure of negative rates and continued bond purchases to provide economic stimulus is refreshing.  The ECB needs a simple lesson in epidemiology – no virus spreads well if it kills its host too quickly. The virus of monetary stimulus uses the banks as the host, but negative rates kill the banks quickly, so the negative rates won’t ever work.  \
- China has shifted from pumping capital out into the world, to needing external capital to fund its economy.  China needs outside investors -- but it still wants to be able to play by its own unique rules.  Note that if China and India start putting in bids for significant amounts of capital to build their countries, that would put upward pressure on interest rates.  In a world where three billion consumers are coming, interest rates don’t stay at the zero lower bound.
- The attack on Abqaiq may be a sign that power is shifting in the Middle East in a way that few understand and that the market isn’t discounting yet.  Saudi Aramco’s spring 2019 debt prospectus showed its largest oil field, Ghawar, is already well into its decline, and recent headlines have begun to take note that US Shale production may soon be in decline as well.

A close reading of events leads to an appreciation of what’s happening but not discounted in current market prices.  As we look for investments, we like opportunities where we can clearly articulate the business or economic opportunity which isn’t in the current stock price -- especially when we are effectively partnering with company management because they are also large shareholders.
In closing, no letter can include everything.  We are open to questions about the inevitable ellipses – both intentional and unintentional.  ***We are students of ******ellipsis******, not its masters.***

## DECEMBER 2019

*The investment business is one of committing your clients' capital to quality growing companies and leaving it there until the company changes or gets grossly overvalued. You ought to have to disclose whether you're in that business or the screwing-around business.*
Robert Kirby, from Wall Street People by Charlie Ellis

### VALUE, GROWTH & PATIENCE
Effective investment management over time requires four key elements: (1) a **point of view** or the set of assumptions and beliefs to help cut through the noise, (2) ** valuation** – buy ownership at a price that’s less than your estimate of the value of the business; (3) **growth** – buy ownership of businesses whose value and total addressable market are both increasing over time; (4) **patience** – when you get a fair price for a growing business, let time and the mathematics of compounding work in your favor.  We have discussed our point of view, valuation, and growth in previous letters.  These first three are not simple, but also aren’t that difficult to articulate.  However, patience – well, that is hard to explain.  When one is patient with an investment it is akin to “standing still like a hummingbird” (Henry Miller).  Patience isn’t passive – it’s an active process.
Robert Kirby is a somewhat surprising advocate of patience.  One of his favorite activities, outside of his efforts building Capital Guardian Trust Co., was racing cars.  But if you understand how having the patience to let wealth compound in a growing business is an active decision – one where you go back and regularly check on the business, its progress and management, well then it isn’t hard to see why Kirby advocated what he also described at times as a passive approach to active management.  The patient investor who selects growing companies, purchased at attractive prices, also has the benefit of lower taxes and transaction costs.  Patience, properly applied, gives you an edge in the race to produce returns in the long run.
Ben Graham said that “in the short run, the market is a voting machine but in the long run, it is a weighing machine.”  As value investors we tend to buy when the market votes are against a company with a solid underlying business franchise and management team or culture. With many of the larger positions in the fund, we are now in a position of waiting for the voting booths to close and the scales to get rolled out.  Many investors will talk about how they do this – but we believe it is more useful to show a few concrete examples from the fund where the voting has gone against us.  Graham and others have many fine quotes, but we believe the real information about an investor is in their transaction ledger. The information isn’t quoted sentiments, but the quoted prices and share counts where the trades were made.

…As a reminder: our reason for investing in a business, or our ***investment hypothesis***, is made up of four general parts: ***business franchise, growth opportunities, ******management****** and valuation***.  The investment hypothesis is *why* we are invested in different businesses.  But, deciding to invest in a company is just the beginning.  Other difficult decisions follow the decision to buy an initial position: *how* much to own and *when* to increase, reduce or eliminate a position, and *what* new facts we learn over time.  The way an investment develops over time is best expressed as a story.

## MARCH 2020

*The great virtue of a free-market system is that it does not care what color people are; it does not care what their religion is; it only cares whether they can produce something you want to buy. It is the most effective system we have discovered to enable people who hate one another to deal with one another and help one another.**   -- *Friedman, Milton. Why Government Is the Problem (Essays in Public Policy)

### WHAT’S GOING ON: SELECTION, ADAPTATION & PROGRESS
The world is confronting a highly contagious and rapidly spreading virus that is on course to become another of several endemic seasonal diseases which include the common cold (also know as human endemic coronavirus) and the flu.  This new virus is much more dangerous than the flu or the common cold, although we do not know how much more dangerous. We have only been observing it for a few months.
In the face of this unknown threat, most of the economies of the world have limited contact between people to slow the spread of the virus.  The limitations on human contact also limit or shift economic activity.  From an investment research perspective, our focus is not on the economic damage, but how people and companies are adapting to the new environment.  ***As investors, we ******seek****** to steer around ******the ******economic****** impairments****** while focus******ing****** on ******what has changed in ******the long******-******term****** potential and opportunities for different businesses******.  We ask ourselves “What is a business likely to look like three to five years from now?”  ***
We are optimists and believe in people and the ability of individuals everywhere to adapt and innovate.  Our long-term optimism is anchored in the economic power of human agency and free markets to enable and coordinate the creation of wealth.  This isn’t an idealistic position – but comes from the brutal experience of investing in markets around the world for decades and realizing that Dr. Friedman is right – free markets are “*the most effective system we have to enable people who hate one another to deal with one another and help one another.”*   But this virus has led to the expansion of the state and limits on individual freedom and agency everywhere.  We believe that state control and interventions limiting human agency destroy wealth in the long-term.  The state does not build wealth over time, it provides a foundation of safety and stability for individuals and companies to build wealth. We expect that three to five years from now the world will have devised multiple approaches to manage the health risk from the coronavirus.  ***The risk which concerns us in the ******long-term****** comes from the expanded role of the state****** and****** the ******emerging ******barriers****** ******to the free flow of commerce, ******capital****** and people.  ***
In response to the crisis, the Merchant Adventure team has been working remotely since March 6th.  We hold two team meetings a day: one in the morning just after the US markets open and one in the afternoon after the US market closes.  Because we set up our systems in the cloud and online to accommodate frequent travel, we were able to quickly shift to working from home.  In the short-term, we have been surprised by how easily we shifted to virtual meetings.  In the longer-term, working remotely results in a loss of efficiency in communication and workflows – for example, when we are making difficult decisions necessary to navigate a crisis, it is better to be talking face to face.

### **SELECTION**: VALUATION SPREADS BLOW OUT
One core element of how we select securities is valuation.  Valuation is the ritual of comparing the current market prices with accounting measures of business performance.  While this is described as a “style” of investing, we do ask ourselves what the people who don’t relate prices to measures of business performance are doing?  I think we all know that without reference to valuations one is not investing but speculating.  Speculation can we wildly successful and profitable. Until, at last – it isn’t.
We have discussed the valuation spreads within the S&P 500 Index (“SP50”) and the MSCI All Country World Index (“ACWI”) before, but its important to update those numbers.  In the first quarter of 2020, stocks in the S&P 500 which traded at over 4x sales declined approximately 9.9% while stocks in the S&P 500 which traded at less than 1x sales declined approximately 36.0%.  ***For the 5 quarters leading up to March 31, ******2020****** the stocks in the S&P 500 which traded at over 4x sales appreciated approximately 25.0% while the below 1x sales stocks declined 24.7%. ******  ***In the last 15 months, the largest valuation gap in the history of the S&P 500 has opened up. It shows no sign of closing.  As a result, we have two markets, not one – the glamour stocks are up 25% and the value stocks are down 25% since December of 2018.  We are invested largely on the wrong side of this valuation gap.
We believe that accounting, cash flows and earnings matter: valuation matters.  When the markets decide earnings matter and focuses on them, the valuation gap will close.

### **ADAPTATION**: MANAGEMENT
No one knows exactly how dangerous this virus will be over time, or how we will manage it. Even though our knowledge of the virus is limited by the fact that it is new, we do get to see how everyone reacts.  How each company is managed in a crisis.  ***This is a real time test of management quality, ******vigor****** and ability to adapt to rapidly changing economic conditions.  ***
***None of the adaptations which ******leading****** portfolio companies made in response to the novel virus were ******actions****** which we anticipated****** when we purchased the securities******, but the adaptations were enabled by qualities and capabilities which were part of our criteria for selecting securities.***  Management matters because management’s job is to identify and pursue new opportunities which emerge.  While we do not know what changes will come out of the current crisis – we do know that the businesses with vigorous leadership who are able to adapt and help their customers adapt will win.  Rather than spending our time predicting the course of the virus, we are looking to fill as much of the portfolio as possible with ownership interests of companies led by management teams who are working to find ways to prosper in the face of the extreme and unanticipated challenges presented by the current crisis.

CLOSING
We will sometimes deliver dramatically different short-term investment results than the market indices because our portfolio is a collection of idiosyncratic businesses, not systematic factors.  Our goal is to beat the market over time by owning shares of growth businesses when they are priced as if they will never grow again. This is traditionally referred to as “value investing” but with the caveat that because of the way today’s economies are driven by innovation and creative destruction, we are loath to invest in declining businesses at any price.
Five years from now, we expect the virus will be behind us and scientists will have invented new tools or have repurposed old ones to address Covid-19 and the subsequent viruses which will come after it.  Although the virus will be behind us, we are likely to be living in a world with an expanded state presence in the economy and our lives.
