Just as Milton Friedman had his way of expressing what’s unique about markets he also captured the real problem with the expansion of the state: “*the tragedy is that because government is doing so many things it ought not to be doing, it performs the functions it ought to be performing badly.*”  The current crisis we are living through is a compound tragedy – the tragedy of both a deadly new disease and the tragedy of world which was caught unprepared for it.  That is what has happened.  Going forward, the question is how we will all adapt.  **
**
## JUNE 2020

### COVID-19: WHY WE NEED TO BE RESILIENT & ADAPT
Responses to the pandemic have varied from totalitarian to laissez faire to tragicomic.  The current judgement of financial markets is that the worst of the turmoil is past.   Responses from both monetary (central banks) and fiscal (legislative or executive) authorities have been swift and of unprecedented magnitude.  The risk to markets at this point is that the government stimulus is in the past, but the financial reckoning with the losses from economic shutdowns is still ahead of us.
Economic shocks (like what we are experiencing now) are disorienting, but they are not mysterious.  They can be a playground for both innovation and opportunism as Bernard-Henri Levy reminds us in The Virus in the Age of Madness.  But, then Stuart Kauffman reminds us that chaos and instability have a structure and logic of their own in At Home in the Universe.  At the end of the day, instability in human affairs is itself often what is temporary and unstable.
We would suggest now is the time for investors to closely review their own investment options or opportunity costs.  We will return to more individual company discussions in future letters, but this quarter seems a useful time to step back and take a broader perspective*.*

*“**Our intuitions about the requirements for order have, I contend, been wrong for millennia.  We do not need careful construction; we do not require crafting.  We require only that extremely complex webs of interacting elements are sparsely coupled.**”** **-- *Stuart Kauffman, At Home in the Universe

Kauffman observed that densely connected networks are unstable and networks with points of sparse connection tend toward stability.  This simple idea links network structure to stability and applies to the networks of transactions which make up companies, industries, and economies. For instance, network stability is one reason that systems tend to consolidate into a few leading companies or political parties, and the chaos and complexity get encapsulated within the organizations.
Disruptive technologies have torn down, or are tearing down, many legacy economic networks.  As Mark Andreessen famously noted, “software is eating the world.” We are only beginning to understand the characteristics of the new networks we have built in their place. At the time of his departure from Instagram in late 2018, Kevin Systrom told Kara Swisher *“Social media is in a pre-Newtonian moment, where we all understand that it works, but not how it works. There are certain rules that govern **it** and we have to make it our priority to understand the rules, or we cannot control it.”*
The defenestration of traditional media by social media is a useful example of how technological change is more nuanced than just “more for less.”  While the traditional media had a myriad of problems, it was sparsely connected and stable; so stable that it had a role in the formal power structure as the fourth estate.  In contrast, we are now struggling through the consequences of “densely connected” social media networks playing a dominant role during a crisis.  Effective response to the novel coronavirus requires coordinated, collective action.  Instead of trusted stable information channels to coordinate responses to the novel coronavirus, our technological progress brings us cacophony and chaos:
*… a collective panic, aggravated by news channels and social networks that, by reporting to us, day after day, the numbers of those who had died, were still in critical condition, or had recovered, herded us into a parallel universe in which nothing else, anywhere, was news—and, in so doing, drove us quite literally mad. After all, that is how Chinese water torture works—the sound of the drop, repeated **endlessly, becomes a fearsome dragon. How would we react if the traffic safety authorities decided to install giant loudspeakers at one-mile intervals to inform us of the day’s highway fatalities?*
Levy, Bernard-Henri. The Virus in the Age of Madness.

The qualitative experience of a merciless assault of noise that Levy captures as the *“sound of the **drop, repeated endlessly**” *-- a description of being tuned in to a dense network of connections, robs us of the ability to consider and calculate.  Substantive debates of trade-offs are ignored, while we seek out news of the latest calamity, the *“**sound of the **drop, repeated endlessly.”** * It is precisely at these critical moments in crises that we must weigh the opportunity costs of the alternatives available to us, and not become distracted.
The economic shocks from COVID-19 and our responses to it will fill history books.  But our task is to manage wealth through the crisis, not to evaluate it after the fact.  As we look out at a world dominated by governments printing money, bonds without yields and private investments trading at high prices for high fees, we continue to believe that publicly traded equities provide the best alternative to protect and build real wealth.   In the process of looking for the best individual companies to protect and build wealth, we also need to make sure that we are aware of our public equity investment alternatives. The decision of what not to buy is just as important as the decision of what to buy.

CLOSING
Markets have gone from expecting a broad economic collapse in March to pricing in a technology led resurgence in July. In this environment, we have focused on our opportunity costs, assessing tradeoffs between what we own and what we could own.  This opportunity cost analysis is analogous to a broader analysis and planning exercise which leaders in different organizations across economic sectors and geographies are either working on or will be working on to prepare for 2021.
A world which flits from one certainty to its opposite may be tinged by madness as Bernard-Henri Levy reminds us, but as Stuart Kauffman carefully explains, our intuitions about order are generally too pessimistic; stability does not require much.  The world naturally finds order despite our fears.

## SEPTEMBER 2020

*A businessman isn’t a mathematician. Remember that. Never become hypnotized by the beauty of numbers. A businessman is someone who buys at ten and is happy to get out at twelve. The other kind of man buys at ten, sees it rise to eighteen and does nothing. He is waiting for it to get to twenty. The beauty of numbers. When it drops to ten **again** he waits for it to get back to eighteen. When it drops to **two** he waits for it to get back to ten. Well, it gets back there. But he has wasted a quarter of his life. And all he’s got out of his money is a little mathematical excitement.*
V. S. Naipaul, A Bend in the River, p.24

The abundance of numbers in the financial markets makes it easy for *businessmen to mistake themselves for mathematicians and become hypnotized by the beauty of numbers*, as Naipaul puts it. One should not ignore the numbers, but the numbers must connect with a narrative or story.  The numbers alone do not mean anything. The story that can be told with the numbers, that is everything.

### MEAN REVERSION IS DEAD, VALUATION ISN’T
The economy evolves and changes.  We all know this – but chasing down what economic changes mean in a way that can inform investment decisions in the present is always a matter of educated guesswork. The structure of the global economy before 1989 was very different than it is now.  In 1989, the Berlin Wall fell and the Eastern Block began to open up to the West, and China continued opening up while clamping down at the same time.  The end of the military confrontation that defined the post WWII period would lead to the commercialization of a host of technologies, including the internet.

Before 1989, public companies enjoyed a privileged level of permanence within the economy. By undertaking the significant investment required to provide a standard of transparency and governance, they could raise public capital. Furthermore, if they were dealt some blow, their scale and status granted them access to funding that would allow time to work in their favor.  This literal margin of safety allowed for the process of mean reversion in returns on capital to be probable. Unless mortally wounded, most companies could recover and reward shareholders for their patience. Mean reversion of returns on capital was a key economic phenomenon that generated returns for value investors.

But American capitalism was also transforming in a process described by John Weir Close as  A Giant Cow Tipping by Savages. The emergence of new classes of investors, new financial instruments and new legal theories impaired the privilege of corporate survival and the economic structure which supported the mean reversion of returns on capital within a corporation over time.  The time of buying statistically cheap stocks and holding them until someone fixed the problems in the business ended.  Venture investors build new competing businesses from scratch that are not burdened by legacy liabilities or bureaucratic org structures. Distressed debt investors profit from bankruptcy through credit default swaps and make money again as the debt converts to equity, resulting in ownership in an unlevered company with lower costs after bankruptcy. There is a profit to be made by investing capital on the opposing sides of an incumbent business – in disrupting (venture) or dunking (distress).  The excess returns in venture and distressed investing come from reducing the returns on capital of the incumbent – preventing mean reversion.  This changing ecology of investors and capital markets instruments is one of two key factors which have killed the mean reversion of returns on capital.

The other factor is the acceleration of technology and innovation. Before the era of rapid technological change, the biggest plant would achieve economies of scale that formed an effective barrier to entry from new capital. In their 2005 book Competition Demystified, Bruce Greenwald and Judd Kahn collapsed Porter’s five competitive forces to one: scale.  But now technological innovation frequently trumps scale.  We keep getting More from Less as Andrew McAfee describes in his 2019 book. As a result, the newest plant or product has the tendency to also have the lowest cost structure; the level of the cost advantage is increasingly a function of improvements in technology and process innovations rather than size. In cash flow terms, capital expenditures do not necessarily sustain profit margins and run the risk of impairment. Innovation creates stranded assets in legacy businesses because the capital cost of new capacity falls so fast that legacy capital investments cannot be recovered.  Stranded assets are assets that will never earn back the capital invested in them.  These assets revert to zero, not some historic average return.

Value investing is not simply mean reversion in returns on invested capital anymore.  It is *“Gret**z**ky at a discount”* – *businesses skating to where the puck is going to be, **while market prices don’t reflect much of an expectation that they can skate.  *To have an idea *where the puck is going to be**, *we need a narrative or hypothesis about how different businesses or segments of the economy evolve and also how individual businesses adapt to economic shocks, competition and other slings and arrows of fortune.

In the narrative, numbers support a story describing a target market, management team, business franchise, financial model, and expectations in current market prices.  We divide market price changes into shifts in (a) investor expectations and (b) company fundamentals on which those expectations are formed. Investor expectations change more frequently and dramatically than company fundamentals.

***Value investing is about buying low expectations, and valuation is the work of measuring the level of expectations in the price of a security.***

CLOSING
One of the greatest risks to an investor is being hypnotized by the numbers which are constantly dancing past us in economic releases, earning releases and price quotes.  The way to avoid being hypnotized by numbers isn’t more numbers, it’s using the numbers to construct a narrative of how businesses and industries are evolving, in order to get in the way of and profit from changes in the economy.  Each of the companies in the portfolio has its own story, which we are happy to discuss.

## DECEMBER 2020

### Management: Nothing Compounds without Leadership
No business ever accomplished exceptional results over time without quality leadership.  Effective leaders build strong organizations and set a vision that endures.  Quality management sounds qualitative, but the magic of good management is that a qualitative vision implemented through a set of practices, processes and norms in a firm end up showing up in the financial statements.  The test for every vision is the future sales and profit growth which it helps create.

### Opportunity: Developing & Developed World Growth
For the most part, innovation drives economic growth in the developed world, while demand growth, mainly from enfranchising new consumers, drives economic growth in the developing world.
Most innovation and productivity improvements now come from the digital transformation of businesses and economies.  We think of the process of digital transformation as moving through four phases: digital model to digital shadow to digital twin to digital source.  The transformation starts with being able to build a digital model of a process or artifact and proceeds to where decisions and actions in the physical world are based on and coordinated by the digital version, the digital source.
We are still very early in this process, and there will be seemingly endless iterations.  One easy way to think of digital transformation is to think of Tesla and Bitcoin as primitive prototypes or instances of digital shadows (2nd stage of 4) of a car and a currency.  A lot of the cloud is an attempt to make a sort of digital shadow of your phone or your computer – but then as we made the digital shadow of the phone and the computer, those two merged into smart phones and tablets in different ways.  The transformation is not just digitization or virtualization, it is that in the process of digitizing one thing it eats other items (phones displaced cameras and clocks and tape recorders and players and many other single use devices) and becomes something else. Part of the digital transformation is refactoring and re-engineering.  The examples are all around us.
Opportunities created by digital transformation will impact every part of the economy and are at the core of economic progress: our ability to *do more with less*.  The challenge is to try to track what the digital transformation means for each industry and company.  The rapid development of COVID vaccines illustrates the digital transformation at work: the rapid formulation of vaccines was enabled by sequencing the virus RNA and then synthesizing RNA sequences to “teach” the human immune system to protect against infection.
The opportunity of fundamental economic growth in the developing world is driven by individuals exercising increased agency in their lives because of increasing wealth: improving technology and reductions in coercion.  *Gapminder* is an excellent resource on global health and how people live in different parts of the world at different levels of wealth.  From 1970 to 2015, the global economy lifted 40% of humanity out of extreme poverty to just above that – existing on $2 to $8 per day, or what Gapminder refers to as income level two.  The opportunity for demand growth in the developing world is that 40% of humanity is not going to stop and be satisfied with living on $2 to $8 a day. The rich world is only a little over 10% of humanity and should expect a lot more people to be joining them in the next 20 years.   These two sources of growth are not mutually exclusive. The most compelling investments will benefit from both digital transformation and economic enfranchisement.
Once we find a business that is “in the way of the future” the next question is if we can get in the way at the right price.  ***Investing is always about the price.***

### Value in Variant Expectations: Prices, Profitability & Growth
When our expectations for a company diverge from what is discounted in the market price, we refer to this as “variant” expectations.  Understanding how our expectations vary from the consensus is essential.  Knowing how our expectations vary is an application of what we refer to as the value equation – or the way in which market prices discount sets of expectations for future growth and profitability.  The work in research and valuation is constructing our own expectations for the future growth and profitability of businesses and comparing that to what is in the price.
What is a dollar of earnings in a business worth?  The question is debated even though the way of calculating it is clear. The present value of a steady profit stream is defined by the value equation:  $v= p / (r - g), where   p = profits, r = required or target return and g = growth of profits.

For today, let us review a few notes on the value equation and how we use it:
- The required or target return is our return -- not a weighted average of what everyone in the capital markets wants. It is the amount of money an investor requires to pursue an opportunity. *This rate has nothing to do with interest rates – we are not borrowing money and trying to pocket a spread. *

- The price to earnings (price to profits) multiple a business is worth to you is defined by 1 / (r - g). If you know your required return and have an estimate of the sustainable growth rate, you can know in seconds what multiple to pay for a business and reach you target return.

- Required return and growth are bounded.  Our required return is always above 12% and our maximum growth rate for a steady state business at scale is 8%. Therefore, our gap between required return and growth is always above 4%, meaning our target long-term P/E multiple will always be less than 25x earnings.  These limits are part of building in a margin of safety.

- Convert the P/E to a price to sales ratio with a "normalized" or "long-term” net margin. Since Profits = Sales x Net Margin, a Fair Price/Sales = Net Margin / (r - g).   Convert a P/E to a price to book ratio using long-term ROE as the measure of profitability: Fair Price/Book = ROE / (r – g).

A key value of this model is its simplicity.  We can sit down as a team and discuss the drivers of value for each investment – everything collapses into a discussion of what management is driving the business toward and the growth and profitability of the business in that state.

***The ******value equation****** is a simple way to organize the complex discussion of how different businesses create value and how the value of a business compares to prices in the markets.  ***For each of our holdings we can explain how and why we expect greater growth and or profitability than the market.  The model defines the relationship between growth, profitability, price, and value.  *Growth is just part of the value equation.*

….The digital transformation of each business has the potential to drive higher future growth and wider margins than the market price of many stocks imply.  But if every company transforms, we must also remember that the competition unleashed by digital transformation, in that case, could drive growth and margins down instead.

# PRIMER LESSON
***Excerpts from ******Notes and Letter******s from 200******2****** to 200******5***

## FEBRUARY 2002
### FALSIFICATION, COMPETITION, VALUATION & INVESTMENT HYPOTHESES.* *
*** ***
***“Oz, left to himself, smiled to think of his success in giving the Scarecrow and the Tin Woodman and the Lion exactly what they thought they wanted. ‘How can I help being a humbug,’ he said, ‘when all these people make me do things that everybody knows can't be done?’” ***
***        Frank ******Baum,  *****The**** Wizard of Oz**** **
*** ******	***
The Wizard of Oz is a great work of American literature, one that I appreciate for putting Kansas (my home state) on the map.  In the quote above, the wizard asks a good question, how he can help being a humbug, “when all these people make me do things everybody knows can’t be done?”

*The markets demand* companies have steadily growing earnings, even though earnings are a residual.  *The media demands *single-state predictions of the future, although science indicates that our world is best described in terms of probabilities. It’s worth noting that what we actually know about the future is that it hasn’t happened yet. Therefore, it cannot be known or definitively predicted.  There appears to be a constant and steady *demand for assurances* and knowledge that we know doesn’t exist, requiring an ample *supply of **humbug*.

NOW WHAT EXACTLY ARE WE CALLING HUMBUG?
For the definition we reach back to an argumentative Austrian, Karl Popper, in the autumn of 1934, when he published the Logic of Scientific Discovery.   While all the other thoughtful folk were asking how to *verify* facts, Popper flipped the question on its head, and asked how to *falsify* them.  Any statement that isn’t falsifiable (and involves opening your wallet) is humbug.

WHAT’S THE DIFFERENCE BETWEEN FALSIFYING & VERIFYING?
Verification is *positive*; falsification is *negative*.  When we verify a theory we try to confirm it or prove it *positively *with observations.  When we falsify a theory, we test whether the observations are consistent with the theory, and reject the theory only when the observations are *negative*.  Falsification does not allow a theory to ever be proven, but provides the criteria for a theory or speculation to be rejected.  The scientific method of testing hypotheses is centered on falsifiability – a theory is never proven, only *dis*proved; it tells us that we *“only know what is not true.”*
*** ***
WHAT DOES THIS HAVE TO DO WITH INVESTING?
Falsification also provides the foundation for long-term investing, because once you realize that you can *“only know what you don’t know,” *the goal becomes avoiding (a) making mistakes, (b) operating on false hypotheses, (c) talking when you should be listening.  The goal is ***negatively*** defined, in terms of ***“what not to do.”***

Warren Buffett of Berkshire Hathaway states this in two ways: (1) the first rule of investing is not to lose the client’s money and the second rule is never to forget the first rule, and (2) one must stay within one’s circle of competence.  Peter Bernstein, the author of myriad investment tomes, observes that investing is a loser’s game, a game in which the winner is the one who makes the fewest mistakes.  Both views are consistent with the concept of falsifiability or avoiding situations that don’t make sense.

The successful long-term investor will focus on what one doesn’t know and how one knows that.  *How* one “knows” what one “doesn’t know” is realized by one’s investment framework.   *** ***

Our investment framework incorporates two basic ideas:** **
- **Competition**** **– Competitive analysis applies at every level of the economy and competition is the key engine behind improvements in productivity and growth that we rely on to improve the standard of living.  We believe *businesses compete for capital and capital competes for returns.  *A company’s competitive plan is synonymous with its strategy – competition defines strategy.
- * ***V****aluation**** **–** **The value of an asset is determined by the present value of the future cash flows to that asset.  The components for determining the value of an asset are well known and widely accepted:
*i**.* the discount rate *ii.* the growth rate *iii.* the profits
The discount rate is our required rate of return on the investment.  Company growth rates cannot deviate from industry growth rates for very long in a competitive industry.  A return on capital framework may be used to reasonably estimate a *competitively* sustainable level of profitability.  (A range of values may be calculated for the cash flows and growth rates to come up with a reasonable valuation range for the security.  A margin of safety is built into the discount rate or our required rate of return.)

We believe that one can increase wealth over time, outperforming the relevant indices, by investing in companies with sustainable businesses that are selling at a discount to our estimate of the present value of their future cash flows.  On particular names we may be right or wrong, but in aggregate, if we are falsifying our hypotheses and avoiding humbug, this should be the case.

HOW DOES THIS ALL COME TOGETHER INTO AN INVESTMENT HYPOTHESIS FOR A COMPANY?
The numbers that we use in the valuation of the security are said to constitute our investment hypothesis for a particular stock. The expected profitability of the company and its calculation method, the required rate of return and the sustainable growth rate of profits, are each part of the investment hypothesis regarding the value of the company.  Whether the value is realized by shareholder will depend on whether the company is able to deliver the projected numbers and whether the market will discount the growth in the price of the stock, or some other combination of company performance and market expectation.

HOW DOES ONE FALSIFY AN INVESTMENT HYPOTHESIS?
Check your estimates of the value of a company by looking at the company’s financial statements.  In a sense, a company’s financial statements are its credit application for a loan from our clients – a loan which the company has no obligation to ever repay, and is sold to us second-hand.  In this “credit application” we first adjust the numbers to reflect the economics of the business, and then look for evidence that the adjusted numbers do in fact support the current valuation.  For every valuation, or market price, there is a set of numbers (discount rate, growth, and profits) that justifies that price.  If we look at the numbers reflected in the price of the stock, given our adjusted perspective on the company’s financials: capital employed, return on capital, growth rate, etc. – and the numbers implied by the price are both credible and lower than our expectations for the company-- we may invest, but not yet.  Next we look at a variety of other metrics over time.  The metrics differ based on the industry, but we are looking for some quantification of what we qualitatively assessed as the company’s competitive position.  As the company and economy change, the investment hypothesis may be modified.***  ***

HOW DOES THIS ALL TIE BACK TO HUMBUG?

As an analyst one ***qualifies***, ***quantifies***, and ***tests***.  Each qualitative observation should be traceable back to a quantitative metric or proxy in the financial statements, otherwise it’s just humbug that we can’t test or falsify*.  Numbers and testing -- “checking the tape” or falsification, under any of these names, is the antidote for humbug*.  This is the antidote that underpins the scientific method and the process of discovery.  Along with competition, this approach to the world has led to our nation’s extraordinary growth.  The same basic logic should underpin the analysis of what goes a portfolio. *And the analysis is never done*.

## APRIL 2002
### THE USE OF MODELS & FRAMEWORKS

We use models to “climb up,” or quantify what we easily can, and then we “throw it away” and try to understand the business issues that are not easily quantified.

To steal a quote from Karl Popper’s arch rival:

*“My propositions serve as elucidation in the following way: anyone who understands me eventually recognizes them as nonsensical, when he has used them – as steps – to climb up beyond them. (He must, so to speak, throw away the ladder after he has climbed up it.) He must transcend these propositions, and then he will see the world aright.” *
Ludwig Wittgenstein, Tractatus Logico-Philosophicus, 6.54

As investors, the conservative and prudent way to use the model is not to make projections, but to use the framework to identify companies for which the market has irrationally low expectations – companies that look like winners but are expected to lose. The purpose of this model is to act as a framework for highlighting these businesses.

### COUNTING, SCORING, FINANCIAL STATEMENTS, AND FALSIFICATION

*“The first step in any analytical approach to credit evaluation is to see precisely what the information on the application and credit report **tell** us.  The way to do this is to identify what information exists and then to count…. The counting process is always most revealing.” *
*An Introduction to Credit Scoring**, Edward M. Lewis, p.27 *

Two of the most important things we each know how to do are count and speak.  Of these two, the one we most often forget – as we enjoy the sound of our own voices, is counting. Counting answers the questions how many, how often, how long etc. Any analytic approach to a problem will rely on data, numbers – what has been counted.  In the case of companies, their credit application to us is their financial statements.

This approach is somewhat similar to what a bank might do before deciding to give an individual or institution a loan, and, just as the bank is the creditor, the investor is a creditor.  Just as the bank tallies up a credit score for a company or individual before giving them a loan, we tally up a score for companies before investing, giving them a long-term loan without a commitment to repay, purchased second-hand.

If we rely on things that we can count, then we will miss some things.  Arguably, the things we will miss will not be real – but the market has a way of turning perceptions into reality by giving companies currency in the form of their stock.  This shadowboxing with perceptions and expectations is not a game we play, although we will take advantage of the game when it offers us securities at a discount.

We will stick with items that we can count in a company’s financials.  Add these counts up into a score, and then double-check.  We will argue over what to count and how to add up the score, but not the value of counting and scoring. After all, without keeping score, how do we know if we won?

The purpose of the model is to provide a framework for testing the hypothesis that certain measurable, observable data from a company’s financial statements, the company’s competitive position and market prices can provide useful investment information.  This theory cannot be proven, but it can be tested and refined, again and again.
** **
## MAY 2002
### EXPECTATIONS, SURPRISES AND VALUE

“Expect the Unexpected”
*– from a Kansas City Kings bumper sticker in the early 1980’s *

The last time the Kings were a contender, they were in Kansas City and their motto was “*expect the unexpected*.”  The “backcourt boozers” never guessed that this meant that they would leave one small market for another, but nevertheless, the motto appears to have relevance in sports, life, and the market.

***Who would have expected that***, as the Enron hearings continue, and their accountant pleads guilty to obstruction of justice – PBS is showing Anthony Trollope’s *The Way We Live Now*, in which a financier swindles 1870’s London in an eerily familiar plot.  The financier, Melmont, silences questions stating that “this is a delicate and complicated business matter” – the same quote used by **both** Mr. Skilling, and now Merrill Lynch.

As we sit in surprise and wonder at the way that life imitates art imitating life adhering to a basketball slogan, it’s important to point out that we are certain of at least one thing: *at this time next year we will have been surprised again*. This is the nature of the beast.  So, we follow the accidental wisdom of the Kansas City Kings and *expect the unexpected*.

This *certainty of surprise* is an important reason that value investing makes sense to us. Why? We know that we will be surprised and would rather be surprised by good news than bad news.   This is a temperamental disposition that also happens to have financial advantages: asset prices tend to increase after positive surprises, and decrease after negative ones.

Since one can estimate the expectations for growth and profitability that are implied by a stock price, one can select stocks that have low expectations in their price.  Alfred Rappaport and Michael Mauboussin explain the math for calculating these “embedded” expectations in their book *Expectations** **Investing*.

The level of expectations in the price of a stock may imply improving profitability, or rapid growth, or both or ***neither***.  The important aspect to remember, as an investor, is that you may suffer a permanent loss of capital if the company fails to achieve the expectations implied in the price of the stock and fails to ever even achieve those expectations again.  Since *we view the permanent loss of capital as ****an**** important risk*, the only way to minimize this risk is to buy low expectations.

The matrix to the below illustrates the problem.  The “+” in represents a gain, the “-“ a loss, and the “0” signifies a draw.  What the matrix illustrates is that the only time one gains is when the actual performance of the company exceeds the expectations in the price.  While one may do somersaults to get around this fundamental fact, an investor who ignores it will suffer the same fate of the salmon swimming upstream who avoids the fishermen, escapes from the bears, but still never gets back to the ocean.

***Expectations***

Low	        High

| **+** | **0** |
| --- | --- |
| **0** | **-** |

High

***Actuals***

Low

The matrix illustrates a one-dimensional example of surprises.  In the real world, companies and the market have many dimensions and the problem is more complex.  But the addition of different dimensions or “factors” such as industry competition, innovation, or economic growth does not change the nature of the problem.

The nature of the problem is that once one has high expectations there is less upside.  High expectations are a set-up for disappointment, just as low expectations are a set-up for a positive surprise.

This way of viewing surprises and expectations is a temperamental disposition, investment style and wealth accumulation strategy which either makes sense to you or it doesn’t.   The temperamental issue is why value investors are born that way, not made.

## FEBRUARY 2004
### CUCKOO CLOCKS & BALD SPOTS: THE PERSISTENCE OF INNOVATION & INDECISION

*And time yet for a hundred indecisions, **And** for a hundred visions and revisions, Before the taking of a toast and tea. ** *
*In the room the women come and go Talking of Michelangelo. ** *
*And **indeed** there will be time *
*To wonder, “Do I dare?" **and,** "Do I dare?" *
*Time to turn back and descend the stair, *
*With a bald spot in the middle of my hair— *
*  **	From The Love Song of J. Alfred Prufrock,  *
*          by TS Eliot ** *

We all sometimes worry about appearances, or chat idly and urgently about nothing.  This is human nature. *The key is not to be frustrated by these **biases, but** learn to profit from them. *
* *
As the War on Terrorism, War on Iraq, War on Afghanistan, War on Drugs and other “wars” drag on, we realize there will be time to worry about gossip and appearances.   The backdrop reminds one of Orson Welles as Harry Lyme in The Third Man:

*“Italy for 30 years under the Borgias they had warfare, terror, murder, and bloodshed, but they produced Michelangelo, Leonardo da Vinci, and the Renaissance. In Switzerland they had brotherly love - they had 500 years of democracy and peace, and what did that produce? The cuckoo clock.”*

As a war criminal on the run, Lyme had a vested interest in romanticizing post WWII Vienna – a grotesque perspective.  Warfare presented opportunity for men like him.  But Lyme also had a point -- *never underestimate human ingenuity in the face of adversity.*

When faced with a challenge, people and organizations often surprise themselves with their ability to adapt.  This ability to adapt is part of the fundamental process that ends up manifesting itself as competition and mean reversion of economic profits.  In the United States this process of competition and mean reversion is more robust than in other parts of the world because we have fewer constraints on the flows of capital and labor.

***Over time, Wall Street’s “hundred visions and revisions” of earnings become noise.  As this noise fades into the background, we expect the most likely surprise to be the extent to which businesses have adapted to their respective markets. ***

This is not to say that we expect a sustained economic boom, but that because of this persistently surprising adaptability, the resiliency of the economy is much greater than most thought possible.

As investors, an understanding of competitive forces and innovation results in our being less optimistic than most in a boom and less pessimistic than most in the bust.  In a boom, we expect high returns to attract capital and competition, thus reducing the returns.  In a bust, we expect innovation to produce surprising efficiencies and support returns.

*While Wall Street looks for the $0.01 in **next** quarter’s earnings, at we will be trying to find the $1.00’s which companies are adapting to earn three to five years from now, but which are not in the price today.  *

In this sense, our goal is to constantly be out-of-step with today’s gossip and appearances, or, in other words – to systematically find profit for our clients in the fear of bald spots and in the comfort of cuckoo clocks.

## DECEMBER 2004
### OBSERVATION & OWNERSHIP: AVOIDING THE “WYLIE COYOTE MOMENT”

*Tolstoy * “believed that only by patient empirical observation could any knowledge be obtained; that this knowledge is always inadequate, that simple people often know the truth better than learned men, because their observation of men and nature is less clouded by empty theories, not because they are inspired...” *Isaiah Berlin, **The Hedgehog and the Fox**, p.46 *

“No one in the history of the world ever washed a rental car.”  *Lawrence Summers *

Observation and ownership are two of the keys of a functioning business, economy or organization.  This is obvious, but often overlooked, or underestimated. Berlin’s analysis of Tolstoy gets at the observation angle, and we’ve all rented a car, but never washed it.

A fundamental advantage of a market economy is that it solicits and incorporates many observations from different parties by allowing them ownership (equity shares) of the profits or losses.  The prospect of profit or loss (ownership) “solicits” the input, and an effective marketplace “incorporates” these observations into prices.

The incremental difference between the value of one individual observation and the informed collective observations of a group *has historically been* a key difference between the United States and much of the rest of the world.  Ownership can be thought of as the difference between how folks treat a rental car and their own car, and in the markets this phenomenon is magnified millions of times.

*A focus on observation and ownership informs our investment process*. We look for companies that recognize their strengths and weaknesses, recognize the changes in the world around them, and take responsibility for adapting to, or shaping those changes.

*The net result of observation and ownership, when they work, is constant learning and improvement within an organization.* This improvement and learning is best understood in economic terms as extending the duration of any competitive advantages, or extending the “competitive advantage period.”  The competitive advantage period is the period over which a company’s returns in excess of the industry average disappear, or the amount of time that it takes for the returns to mean revert.
* *
The results of observation are not something that shows up as a separate line item on a financial statement.  It shows up in the sustainability of a business and the stability of exceptional financial performance, which one would expect to be competed away.

Managements that do not observe, adapt, and take ownership (responsibility) for profits and losses are headed for a “Wylie Coyote moment.” That’s the moment when they run past the edge of a cliff, look down, only to find nothing there but air (often hot) supporting their existence.* *
* *
*This Wylie Coyote Moment can be thought of as when a competitive advantage unexpectedly disappears.  *

One way to protect against the “Wylie Coyote moment” is to find management that is an owner.  In other words, where *ownership isn’t a perspective that they adopt in decision-making, it is who they are*.

Aligning Observation and Ownership, in this sense, is a principal-agent problem.  Management is the agent that we want to behave as an owner -- driven by empirical observations about their business.  If management is in fact an owner, then this lowers the cost of getting them to behave like an owner (dilution from options or complicated contracts) and solves one problem in analyzing the company and its incentive structure.

This facet of ownership, while not on the balance sheet, is in the annual proxy statement filed with the SEC.  We refer to firms where management is also a significant owner as “*owner-operators*” and we tend to prefer owner-operators as investments.

Another, more traditional, way to identify companies whose business is driven by principles of observation and ownership is to listen.  In the manic world of Mr. Market, simply listening, taking notes, and comparing what folks said they would do, with what they did is a powerful tool:

- How are shortfalls of surprises explained?
- Who takes responsibility/ownership?
- *How are plans modified **for the** unexpected, and does it make sense?  Or would it only work in a cartoon?*

Understanding the *differences between perceptions and reality* both within the market and within organizations which produce surprises, price shifts or “Wylie Coyote moments” can be a key source of competitive advantage for an investor.

We spend a significant amount of our time observing companies and *doing our best* to either avoid or exploit the Wylie Coyote moments – as we endeavor to invest the core of the portfolio in businesses run by folks who understand the role of observation and ownership in the operation of an excellent business.

## MARCH 2005
### FIRST RATE FUNDAMENTAL ANALYSIS: PLUMBING LEAKY ABSTRACTIONS
* *
“**All non-trivial abstractions, to some degree, are leaky.  **
Abstractions fail. Sometimes a little, sometimes a lot. There's leakage. Things go wrong.
It happens all over the place when you have abstractions.”
Joel Spolsky, The Law of Leaky Abstractions
www.joelonsoftware.com/articles/LeakyAbstractions.html

“The test of a first-rate intelligence is the ability to  hold two opposed ideas in
the mind at the same time, and still retain the ability to function.”
F. Scott Fitzgerald, The crack up.

First rate fundamental analysis is about finding two or more opposing facts, and finding how it is that both are true.  It is not just functioning while holding two opposing ideas in the mind at the same time but actually being energized and motivated by the paradox.

Fundamental analysis may be taught but good analysts aren’t taught.  They were made that way.

The paradoxes that the fundamental analyst seeks are often a result of leaky abstractions.  When we abstract, we inevitably simplify, loosing something of the description of a specific item to extend the description to a larger set of items.  Computer programming relies on abstraction, just as accounting does.  All languages – whether in the context of business transactions or bits and bytes, are based on systems of abstraction.  Thus they all leak.

But you probably don’t think – wow – what was leaky about that last sentence I uttered, or that last transaction I made.  But in some fashion a good analyst is thinking about precisely what information the price mechanism, or other language, has missed.

The prices on the stock exchange, commodity and currency exchanges represent a mix of real and speculative activity riding on top of consumer, business and government transactions described in financial statements and economic data.  If you think family gatherings can be a mess of miscommunication – think of the different economic participants as family members with a 2 word vocabulary: buy and sell – all shouting simultaneously.

SO WHERE DOES THE ANALYST BEGIN?
With a hypothesis, which is translated into a search of either qualitative or quantitative sources. For example, one might begin with simple screens, looking for two opposing ideas present in the data associated with one company, such as companies with rising dividend payments and decreasing or flat stock prices.  Find me companies with lower expectations in the stock price than they have had in the past, but which still have strong cash flows, balance sheets, etc...  The conflicting ideas are the expectations in the current price versus the company’s historical or expected financial performance   Then we go through the list to sort the “probably will be’s” from the “has been’s.”

But you can’t be fixed to one screen or another. You have to be fixed to the structured, deliberate and unceasing search for apparent contradictions.  The apparent contradictions hide information that the abstractions have lost – data that has leaked out.

Leaky abstractions in accounting come from the application of the matching principle.  Matching is the principal underlying accrual accounting which seeks to match expenses and revenues with each other in time.  This is different from cash accounting, which seeks to match the amount of cash in the drawer or account.  There is no necessary wrong or right here.  Just that what actually happens in the cash drawer or account will diverge from the accrual accounting – in depreciation, amortization, capitalization & expensing.

AND WHERE DOES THIS LEAD?
You never know.  Generally, to some interesting reading, and conversations.  Always to some boring reading and boring conversations.  And occasionally to investable ideas.  The hit rate is well below 50%.

WHAT MAKES AN IDEA “INVESTABLE”?
The classic line which we have used is “differentiating” our perspective from Wall Street.  When our perspective on a company is different than what we are reading, and different than what’s in the price, we lose some sleep, and take another look.

We are not looking for the “sure thing.”  We are looking for opportunities in which the potential and probable upside is significantly greater than the potential and probable downside.  Quantitatively, if you can get a good sized set of economically independent companies which have more than twice as much upside potential as downside risk, one does just fine.  So that’s the basic probabilistic backdrop.

A FINAL PARADOX
Investable and profitable are two different things.  The profitability of an investment is not in our control.  We are price takers.  That’s the structure of the market.  We don’t control the prices that get flashed at us.  We do control whether we take the price.  This is another of those paradoxes.  You don’t control the price, but nothing matters more than controlling yourself in the face of prices -- price taking discipline.

So first-rate fundamental analysis is about dealing with and reconciling contradictions that begin and end with prices.  Finding the leaks in the price mechanism.  We are just plumbers after all.

We’ll be here plumbing the depths of the markets and market prices – hoping to profit from and occasionally help fix the leaks.

# PART 2

PRINCIPLES
&
PROCESSES

# EXCELLENCE

Time will tell if the fund achieves excellence, but that is our unrelenting goal.  Although he has passed away, Bloom left clear thoughts on mediocrity:

*… we certainly owe mediocrity nothing, whatever collectivity **it** *
*purports to advance or at least represent.* (How to Read and Why).

Collective forms of mediocrity dominate the current business environment, from index funds to negative interest rates.  We believe that one should be careful of these institutionalized forms of mediocrity.  Blindly relying on them for your future wealth and investment returns might not work as well as the crowds suggest.

Excellence is idiosyncratic.  There isn’t a formula for it – it’s another reverse Anna Karenina problem: “mediocrity is all alike; excellence emerges in its own way.”  The only path I know to excellence is to reject mediocrity.  Rejecting mediocrity takes different forms, but there must be a persistence to it. A persistent and consistent drive for*** what’s next***.

The way to excellence brings us back to the original principles of focus and imagination.  We use our shared imagination to catalog possibilities of what’s next in business, the economy and specifically for our companies.  Then we focus.  Focus drives the persistence, day after day – learning about the companies and events, refining our knowledge and processes.  We focus on both executing on our business and on what’s next for the businesses we are invested in.

In the context of our work excellence is indicated by:
- ***Being able to communicate consistent with the Johnny Cash Principle:***
*sharp like a razor, steady like a train.*
High performance requires clear communication.  Discipline and simple shared frameworks are the foundation of effective communication.
***(build high performing teams) ***

- **Knowing the Map of the Portfolio and Companies, **
*from **i**nvestment **t**hes**e**s**,** to **a**dd/**t**rim **prices**,** to **a**reas of **i**nnovation and **c**onvergence*
Know the thesis and it’s three components for each holding, and link that to the tactical add/trim prices through growth and profitability targets, then go back up to how each holding and thesis fits into how the economy and businesses are evolving.  Insight comes after mastery.
***(******seeing what others don’t******)***

- **Staying on the Front foot:**
*always learning and asking and listening to figure out what’s **next*
excellence orients toward possibilities and engages,
to pick possibilities to focus on understanding or making real.
***(allocati******ng****** time and attention)***

# FOCUS & IMAGINATION

Focus & imagination are the two mental modes we use in analyzing companies.  When we zoom into the details – we are focused.  Without focus we cannot see the granular details of events and calibrate our expectations.   When we zoom out to look for what’s not on the page or what’s not in the price – that requires imagination.

I've been struggling with how to create a structured, shared imagination. It isn't enough to just say that we use our imagination, or we see what's not on the page.  If we just do imagine we get the tower of babel problem (Bible) or private language problem (Wittgenstein) – where everyone is speaking in different languages or using similar words but assigning different definitions to them.  Then we have the traditional logical problems too, which boil down to framing expectations in a falsifiable way so that we can test our assumptions (Popper).  We can’t generate useful insights if we can’t test the possibilities we imagine in some way.

This last bit is the key: what actually defines any creative process is the set of limits or gates or constraints (an idea derived from Kauffman).  In Kaufman's work on complexity and networks, order emerges from chaos through binary gates or binary reactions. In research, falsifiability is the structure we use for gating imagination, for testing hypotheses.  Falsifiability or testability forces a structure of clearly defined terms and common objective – solving the other structural problems of not having a shared language for the shared imagination.

Because we are investors, falsifiable ideas need to be defined in the language of business: accounting.  We quantify imagined futures in terms of how they would play out in the financial statements of companies.  Our task is not to understand Popper or Wittgenstein or Kauffman here (although I would recommend it - because doing so results in being able to see things like Feyman in the last reading in the guidebook.).   ***Our task is to imagine what ******“******progress******”****** may look like out ******5 years, 10 years, ******20 years and ******then ******walk it back analytically.  ******W******e start with a broad imagination and keep working at it until parts of the puzzle come into focus****** as financial statement impacts******. ***

***Seeing what's not on the page is the process of seeing where the future has already arrived and then thinking through how it will be "distributed" to the rest of the world******, and then asking what may arrive next and repeating how it might be distributed, and so on******. *** Imagination it iterative.  We have the key advantage of living in one of the parts of the world where the future seems to arrive earlier than most places -- this is a critical advantage for us.  If we combine the advantage of seeing the future arrive with valuation discipline and analytical skill -- we should be able to create an incredible amount of wealth for our clients over time.

This shifts our focus from short-term economic cycles or product cycles or political cycles to long-term progress.  We must not get stuck thinking only of the world as it is.  ***We are investing in the world as it will be, and we are attempting to study how it gets from "here" to "there******.******"***
*** ***
***The process of****** a******ssembling sets of****** long-term expectations and studying how they might play out in the next ******10-******20 years and then walking th******em****** back****** to today****** is generally referred to as "******futuring******.” Building these ******scenarios****** is****** the imaginative part of ******our****** investment process focused on human progress. ***

# HIGH PERFORMING TEAMS

All high performing teams have three things:
Shared Goals:  a common target or purpose
Shared Language: a common way of making sense of the world and communicating
Shared Practices: common ways of doing the work

We commonly refer to these “shared beliefs & behaviors” in an organization as corporate culture.  Which is accurate, but I prefer the more traditional anthropological term for culture – a shared imagination.  High performing teams and winning cultures have a shared imagination that gives them an advantage.
By creating shared sets of goals, language and practices – a group of people removes the normal costs to working together by sharing common goals or targets, sharing language or models of how to make sense of information, and by sharing practices or ways of doing work.
These normal costs of normal people interacting inspired Sartre to write the play No Exit, in which a character famously observes that “hell is other people.”  The difference between having a functional shared imagination versus not having it can feel like the difference between heaven and hell.
Without an efficient shared imagination, no individual can do anything significant.  Any significant work requires teams and teams require culture and shared imagination to perform well.

For Merchant Adventure to build a culture and organization of multiple inter-locking high performing teams, we need to establish and defend the shared goals, language and practices which will enable us to achieve excellence.  This guidebook is a significant step in the process of developing our culture – shared language and shared practices.  A complete Merchant Adventurer’s Guidebook will be a summary of our shared language and shared practices.  This is an essential step in building a high-performance culture, and organizational excellence.

# ETHICS – THREE SIMPLE PRINCIPLES

When I started in the investment business, I remember being intimidated, scared and confused by all the ethics rules.  I understood their purpose – but the complexity of some of the details seemed illogical and confusing.  And in all honestly, the CFA program was a promulgator and the industries bad actors seemed to be advocates of the complexity – which allowed them to break the spirit of the rules with bending their letters.
While we are required to observe the forms implemented by institutions with fundamentally compromised interested and captured boards – the key from my perspective is for us to adhere to our own key principles which are more prescriptive and definite.  The clients interests always come first – but the clients interests are an abstract and legally defined concept and different clients have different interests – so this is complex.

In actual daily actions, simple principles are useful guides.  Ethics gets a bad rap because of the way it has been strangled and bent by lawyers, but good ethics are essential and good ethics are pragmatic and made up of simple rules or principles.
Imagine an action “X”, and you aren’t sure if you should do “X.”  The three specific rules are useful to keep in mind and which we should all check our conduct against are:
- Values: Is “X” who I want to be?  Is it consistent with my values and who I want to be?
- Reciprocation: How would I feel if someone were doing “X” to me or in my name?
- Reputation (Wall Street Journal Rule): Would you want it published in the WSJ that you did “X”?

The only firm which I have worked for which did a good job with ethics was the old pre-2000 Factset Research Systems.  At that time, all new employees sat down with the CFO Ernest Wong and he explained the Wall Street Journal Rule, and explained that we would all be handling a lot of sensitive information for different clients and it would be impossible to anticipate all the potential situations we might find ourselves in – but we needed to be empowered to take care of the clients and along those lines Ernest emphasized that we should just keep in mind the Wall Street Journal Rule and if it felt like we were being asked to do something we wouldn’t want published in the WSJ, then don’t do it and the firm will have your back.  This was the first and only experience I ever had with an executive explaining ethical principles as a way to empower a group of employees and also as a way to make a promise to the people in the firm.  Everywhere else, I had seen ethics as a control structure and as an instrument that effectively created fear and confusion more than it guided any conduct in the interest of clients or business standards.

From my perspective, the proper understanding of ethics and implementation of an ethics program creates a set of rules which primarily empower employees.  By centering on clear principles to guide conduct, ethics becomes empowering – while still serving its other purpose as a control structure.

# COACHING [KATA]

The coaching kata is the habit, or practice, of coaching one another toward excellence.
Each of us has different strengths.  This is the nature of any group, and as Ricardo famously observed, the groups that compete most effectively focus on their strengths (he didn’t exactly say that – but that is my interpretation of why competitive advantage works).
If you have a strength, it is your responsibility to the group to coach others to help them understand and then either defer to you in that area or, the more difficult and more valuable path, for you to help build your strength in an area into a strength of others in the organization.
Coaching isn’t a role – but a mode of interaction.   One key aspect is that we don’t believe that one person is the coach on all things.  I may coach one skill and be coached on another.  Coaching is a mode and the role each of us plays depends on the skill.  We are not either “mentor” or “mentee” in a relationship – we are mentor or mentee in teaching or learning a specific skill or domain.  The roles flip because we each have more weaknesses than strengths.
Mastery, or the ability to coach a skill, requires a lot of work and experience.  For an individual to achieve the mastery to teach one skill well is itself exceptional.  And that is just one skill, and it means that the “master” is mediocre at a host of other things.  Being excellent in one area always means under developing other skills or missing other opportunities.  That is how time works – we only have so much of it to allocate.  The awareness of those sacrifices is humbling – a stark reminder of our limits.
Coaching is a mode of interaction, but to be a coach is to develop skill and awareness, striving for excellence and sacrificing other opportunities.  To be coached is also to be aware of your weaknesses and open to improving them.  The underlying mindset to be able to coach and be coached requires a desire for excellence, humility, and a curious open mind.
Coaching and learning are at the core of continuous improvement.  The practice, habit or kata of continuous improvement is core to building a culture of excellence.  Mike Rother describes the habit in the Toyota Kata reading and also in his books on how Toyota has built a culture of continuous improvement and industrial excellence.

# CALLS. MEETINGS & INTERVIEWS
We have five principles for all calls, meetings and interviews – research oriented or otherwise.  Our fundamental long-term orientation, curiosity about the world and people, and focus on possibilities from the present forward motivate these principles.
In each call and meeting we may have some tactical objective or important facts to check.  These principles do not preclude fact-checking but highlight that fact-checking should never be the entire purpose or communication and although it may periodically by the primary or motivating purpose for a call – it shouldn’t be the primary motivation for most calls or meetings.
Each conversation is an opportunity to learn, build relationships and differentiate who we are as investors.  These principles are designed as reminders of that opportunity:
- ***Use your voice:***  People have sensitive radar and the way in which you speak and how you ask specific questions all must be in your own voice.  So while I offer examples – those are in my voice and the way you might ask questions with the same content and intent will probably be different. The goal isn’t for us all to ask the same questions, but for us to have common principles and use those to shape questions we each ask in our own voice or own way.

- ***Establish Rapport & Context***:  *Ask the person you are speaking to more broadly about their interests and their career*.  You have to genuinely be curious, and doing some research on executives can be helpful here – but it is more important to be genuinely curious and listen actively.  The benefits of this process are that we get some context for strengths and weaknesses in someone’s perspective which is analytically useful and we also establish rapport and ideally begin building a friendly relationship and create a different tone for conversations with Merchant Adventure.  For an early call with someone – this is more important than any facts they may tell you – because without context, how do you know what to make of what they tell us?

- ***Long-term Focus***: If we have a dialog going with a company, the real value of that dialog is in understanding what lessons they learned from the past and what their long-term plans are for the future.  Our questions about the business will always avoid the near past or near future (narrow parts of the cone of possibility – where we can get a lot of that information from sell side analysts I we want it, and what we can’t it is probably illegal for us to ask directly from the company). * Part of how I introduce myself is to say that I really don’t care about any of the next 6 months or last 6 months developments in the context of a call or meeting other than to quickly fact-check.  If I have the chance to talk with companies and executives what I want to focus on with the time is who they are, how they think about the business and where they see the risks and opportunities in the long-term.*

- ***Scuttlebutt / Competitive Perspectives***: Once a dialog is established, ongoing conversations with companies often provide the best information on their competitors or adjacent industries (supplies and customers).  One fun question to ask in different ways once we have a conversation going with a company, is to ask for their thoughts on competitors or what’s they see happening around them.  This can be done in different ways – it can be as simple as, “what’s going on?” or be a creative combination drawing on each of the principles here and asking, “who or what businesses, current or present, do you most admire/respect/learn from?  Which ones really surprised you but in a sort of ‘wow, that was brilliant’ way?”  or something like that.

- ***Take notes***: Take notes on the call.  But remember the notes are for communicating to the team following the Johnny Cash Principle for communications – *sharp like a razor, steady like a train**. *Meeting notes should be a series of 4-5 bullet points. If, as an analyst you realize there is more to digest from the call, then that is a longer separate note, which can be linked to the meeting note – but not part of the meeting notes.

It took me a number of years to realize that a key professional skill as an analyst and portfolio manager is interviewing.  Interviewing is an art, not a science – and the only way to learn it is to practice.  That’s said, there are a number of excellent books on Interviewing which are useful for putting lessons into context.  Useful resources on interviewing include (in no particular order):
- The Craft of Interviewing, Brady (older but still good – focused on writers and journalists)
- Interviewing, Sedorkin (recent – focused on writers and journalists)
- Liespotting, Meyer (useful summary of signs of deception or evasion)
- Infliuence, Cialdini (older, but updated – general principles of human psychology / interaction)
- Make It Stick, Brown (focuses on learning and psychology, but useful - being remembered is important)

Developing our skills as interviewers and differentiating ourselves in the process is another of the areas where we can achieve a competitive advantage.  To win in our competitive business we need to capitalize on every opportunity for advantage.

# TEAM INVESTMENT DISCUSSIONS

As the team grows the way we structure team discussions will evolve.
For now, while we are a small team, there are three key principles for us to keep in mind – one for focus, one for imagination, and one for both:
- Johnny Cash Rule: Sharp like a razor, Steady like a train.
- Freestyling: Changing Directions without Judging.
- Focused on the Possible, the Present: Cone of possibility.
In the way we discuss things we should always seek to share information and make our points as succinctly and powerfully as possible – sharp like a razor, steady like a train.  That’s the Johnny Cash Rule.
When we explore and question each other’s ideas each step in the discussion should be an “and” – plussing.  Discussion proceeds by taking an element of the previous idea and building on it.  This structure comes from improv.  The key to this is to deliberately follow a structure designed to naturally build a shared imagination or where our ideas can build off of each other and we can build off of each other’s ideas.
Finally, focus and imagination.  We look at the possible from the point of view of the present.  Not what could be or should or could have been done or should have been done – but what do we expect from today forward based on what we know now.  And what would we like to know more about tomorrow?

# COMPANY & MEETING NOTES

Notes are useful as communication tools and to archive what we were thinking at a point in time.  Like our communications in meetings, the key principle to keep in mind comes from the man in black:
***….s******harp****** like a razor steady like a train.***

The use of notes in our process is for
communication,
reflection and
calibration.

Notes should not be in the form of minutes, or a complete summary of attendees – unless that is specifically legally required.  The standard model of notes that we all learned which was to summarize information and document events comes from legal requirements and due diligence and prioritizes a sort of comprehensiveness over being comprehendible and useful in practical decision-making.

***Within our analytical framework for a company, note-worthy observations must be clearly linked to elements of the investment thesis (business, management) and any impact on expected growth or profitability (valuation), as well as expected future events (scenario process).  This is the complete logical set and the structure of our approach.  ***
Observations not directly and concisely linked are of unclear relevance. but more importantly when we don’t make the connections we are being “sharp like a spoon, steady like a mule” … which isn’t an efficient or effective way to work.

# STRUCTURE OF POSSIBILITY & ITS IMPLICATIONS

A lot of our models are just about limiting our energy and research to the possible and probable.  The most powerful model in doing this is just to understand the basic structure of possibilities always starts from now.  This point in time.  That’s it.  But think about how much time you hear people spending traveling through possibilities that are impossible because they are about the past.  If we can focus our energy and analysis on the possible world – this can be a source of analytical advantage.

## CONE OF POSSIBILITY
The basic idea in terms of the physical constraints of our universe are simply explained in Hawking’s Brief History of Time in Chapter 2: Space and Time, where he introduces the idea of light cones and this idea is expanded on elsewhere and can be thought of in our context, as “cones of possibility.”  Functionally, the light cone is the cone of possibility because the fastest that information can systematically travel is at the speed of light.
Hawking illustrated it as shown below.

There is an area in the past which can influence the present and then the present is a point, and events in the present can then go on and expand out to influence events in an expanding space in the future.  It’s a very simple idea and it’s just how we understand reality works – with the speed of light as a hard limit.  The key is that you can’t even pretend to analyze the possible unless you make sure you are sitting in the present, and an accurate picture of the present is like a focal point for understanding the past and the future.
Now, I know thinking about the basic structure of the universe and the speed of light constraints seem like odd principles to introduce in a discussion of investment principles – but the next time you catch yourself thinking about what someone should have done – remember that you are asserting a strange ability to change the physical laws of the universe.  Whereas if you were to ask about the impacts of what they did or what they learned and what they may do next, you would be asking logical questions of analytical value and without the absurdity of needing to change the physical laws of the universe.  A simple awareness of the structure of reality offers useful boundaries & tools for every analyst.

## SPEED LIMITS
The first question it should raise is that once we see an event, its impact ripples out in space and time at some rate.  The rate it travels out into society and through human institutions is never the speed of light – there will be other constraints and limits based on the institutions and existing belief structures and how the new event or information relates to existing institutions and belief structures.
Some institutions, like regulatory institutions or self-regulatory organizations, are literally in place to control or limit the spread of impact from events in different areas of human endeavor, to protect the larger society.  Many innovative businesses create economic value by evading these regulatory structures.  This question about diffusion and the shape of the cone of possibility going out from a current event is there in the structure of reality and if we pay attention to this structure, it can lead us to the key questions to ask.

## IMPLICATIONS – OPPORTUNITY COSTS & ALL CASH PORTFOLIOS
The structure of reality also tells us that our choice – our only choice – is what we do in the present.  This is obvious but it is also not an explicit and required assumption of a lot of decision frameworks, which is really weird and wrong.
The most obvious example of this is all of continuous finance which assumes continuous access to credit.  Note that to make the continuity assumption at all you assume that in some present where you begin your model all of the future in which you will be operating will not experience a discontinuity in any key markets.  So you make a decision in the present based or present conditions being largely continuous in the future.  It’s just wrong – look at the cone.  While reality is a relevant long-term constraint, the paradox of social institutions is that we have the ability to create our own legal fictions and shared imaginations which frequently deviate from and make assumptions that violate the structure of the physical world.  This isn’t actually a problem – but just part of being complex emergent phenomena in a creative universe – or that’s probably how someone like Kauffman would look at it.  Our job is to play around the edges of the possible.
In terms of our portfolio and our positions – the edge of the possible is to look at the opportunity costs of holding one position versus another based on everything we know today, and to aggregate all that information into a set of recommended weights for the entire portfolio based on what we know today.  We frequently refer to this as our “all cash portfolio” exercise.  In practical terms, the all cash portfolio exercise is a way to challenge all the positions in the portfolio which were based on past decisions which made sense at the time but which we may or may not have re-evaluated.  What would we buy today if someone hired us to manage an account which was all cash?  And comparing that to our portfolios which are all necessarily aggregations of past buy and sell or past decisions.  The all-cash portfolio exercise is about pulling ourselves and our portfolios into the present – into the focal point of the cone, and a way to shed the past and get rid of analyses anchored in the past.
I know John’s logic for the all-cash portfolio is practical, and I agree with the pragmatics.  But it is also important to be able to see how best practices and pragmatic patterns also fit elegantly into a broader set of principles.  Where we don’t see these simple rules or frameworks and how they are guiding us, we don’t understand what we are doing (Kelvin’s exhortation to quantification).

## THE PRESENT IS ALWAYS THE FOCAL POINT
One of our key principles as a firm is focus, and the structure of possibility is such that the present is always the focal point of possibilities.  This doesn’t mean that we focus on just what is happening in the present but it means that we are always looking at the impact of events in the past present and future through the lens of what we know in the present.  The perspective and awareness of always operating in the present and thinking about how events diffuse through space and time gives us a structure to bring the whole picture into focus.
# COMPOSITION OF ECONOMIC GROWTH
The rule of thumb I remember from early in my career was that economic growth was a mix of unit growth and productivity/innovation, and that mix was roughly 50/50. The unit growth was driven by demographics while the growth in excess of that was called innovation or productivity. Economists weren’t really sure how to define innovation or productivity other than or say it was the part that wasn’t explained by other numbers.
But if we look at the world today, population growth in most of the developed world is a thing of the past.  Peak child for the world, the most children ever, was way back in 2007.
The doubling rate of global population, from 2 to 4 billion people was 25 years and 4 to 8 was then 42 years (slowing dramatically since the 1970s) and we are now at 8 billion and likely to peak around 8.8 billion people within 25 years (the 12 billion number is old bad math).
The composition of economic growth can’t be the old 50/50 anymore.  Looking at the math - for most of the world growth is now entirely an innovation game - the demographics are flat to negative.
Looking at the last decade in the US, we have been shifting to low population growth and gently aging.  Arguably, the shift in capital to tech in the last decade was part of the markets reacting to this shift in the composition of economic growth.
***The key is that the composition of economic growth going forward will not be what it was in the past.  Since as investors we are looking for companies driving economic growth and profiting from it – this ******is a big deal******.***
A lot of our assumptions around the developing world are based on unit growth and income level convergence.  If the unit growth isn’t there and the convergence is mediated by digital platforms as part of a digital path to development, then where we look for opportunities in the developing world fundamentally shifts.
One avoids developing world places and developed world places with negative demographics because those societies are failing (see Moffett in the readings).  This is Russia and East Asia.
One focuses on innovation as the necessary component since it will be the major driver of economic growth and the best innovators which achieve scale will then be in position to get unit growth - so unit growth comes for free with innovation but doesn’t exist on its own in a stable population world.  Innovation doesn’t necessarily mean high tech - but it does mean best in class processes and efficiency and visionary leadership and empowered/engaged employees, which will involve digitization and the digital path in production, distribution and sales.
The composition of economic growth going forward won’t look like the past. Most societies have stopped growing in population, so there can’t be a balance between unit growth driven by population growth and growth driven by innovation.  What does this mean?  Our process should focus on the remaining factor driving economic growth -- if we want to get in the way of where the world is going / future economic growth.

## ECONOMIC GEOGRAPHY
The way we think of economic geography leads us to focus on a surprisingly focused group of countries as attractive target markets and places where we can effectively deploy capital.
