We have three basic criteria for a country to qualify as an attractive economic geography to invest: (1) that they have infrastructure for investment capital and connections to the global economy through a mega city, (2) that the country is large enough to support scale economies in its domestic markets, (3) that the country isn’t shrinking in population.

- Mega-cities, Domestic Scale, Capital Infrastructure: Economic growth and innovation tends to be clustered in mega-cities around the world.  Open Capital Markets: pricing, interest rate and currency infrastructure which we can deploy capital through.
- Domestic population need to be large enough to support economies of scale.
- Population Growth – Immigration & Births:  Successful societies add new members from neighboring states and/or new births.  Failing societies create conditions where people don’t have children and want to leave.

*Countries with over 40 million in population, positive 5-year average population growth through 2021, open capital markets and at least one mega city are: *
***	******	******	******Brazil******, Indonesia, ******Mexico******, South ******Africa******, ***
***	******	******	******Turkey, United Kingdom, United States.***
*Most countries are shrinking, have smaller domestic markets or limited and conditional investment markets. *

***The countries below meet all the criteria above, except they have certain capital market or government restrictions that cause us to exclude them from our open markets list:***
***Argentina, Bangladesh, Democratic Republic of Congo, Egypt, Ethiopia,***
***India, Iran, Iraq, Kenya, Nigeria, Pakistan, Philippines, Vietnam.***
Our hope is that over time the capital markets in this longer list of countries open up.  In the near term, the best way to invest in countries with promising human geography, but closed or restricted capital markets, is to look for quality country fund managers, or country specialists.

***(Country lists based on data from the World Bank – need to write up specific ******processs******)***

# IDEA GENERATION
There is no magic to investment idea generation and evaluation.  There is just a little logic and a constant flow of work.  The purpose of an idea generation discipline or process is to use simple logical rules to limit the scope and define the order of tasks in reviewing a constant flow of new ideas, while also keeping up with all the existing holdings, emerging technologies, and global trends.  Logically, the idea generation is one-part unstructured imagination and two-parts structured focus.

## UNSTRUCTURED & IMAGINATIVE
The unstructured and imaginative part of idea generation is easily described. There isn’t much that can be said – these are the ideas that come from reading, or companies that keep coming up unexpectedly in conversations.  We should allocate some time and attention to “looking out” for the unexpected in unstructured inquiry.  We should be curious about the world and this unstructured part of the idea generation process can be thought of as curiosity time.
But since unstructured inquiry doesn’t impose its own limits, time spent in this mode should normally be 20% of the time any of us spend doing research.  While we don’t allocate much time to this, ideas and observations from an unstructured inquiry are allowed to build up over whatever period of time is required for us to build a structured perspective and feed into the structured part of the process if appropriate.
Our interest in the role of nuclear energy is a recent example of something that knocked around in the unstructured inquiry space as being of interest but without structure for the last year, a time during we were just learning little by little as we watched events and read about the companies and the science.  During that time, we were able to build enough fundamental knowledge of nuclear energy and merge that with our perspective of future energy demands as well as the companies operating in the space, such that going forward we have a structure for researching developments in nuclear energy and the companies in that space.  Over time, themes and companies will emerge from the unstructured space and move into more structured systematic inquiry or they will get discarded.

## STRUCTURED & FOCUSED
The structured part of idea generation is one part qualitative: ideas that come up while researching existing holdings (maintenance research) and quantitative: ideas that come from security screening.  Each of these is discussed in its own section below.
### MAINTENANCE RESEARCH
While keeping up with existing holdings or other companies of interest, we listen to quarterly calls and conference presentations; talk to analysts, management teams and experts; read trade journals and books and newspapers.  And while we are learning about a subject company, we are also learning about its competitors, and suppliers and clients.  Many new ideas come up while keeping up with or refreshing old ideas.  There’s not a single way this happens.
As I tell young analysts, just because you start researching one company doesn’t mean that you will still be researching that company in the end.  If you find another company that seems more interesting during your research, that isn’t a mistake or a problem – it means you learned something. That’s the point of research.
Ideas noticed during maintenance research are sort of like motion you see in your peripheral vision.  You will be focused on learning about one company – but by focusing on that company, you will also start to see how everything is moving around it and relates to that company.  At this point, if you notice that a supplier or competitor or customer has power over the company you are focused on, it should be natural to shift focus to understanding how and why another business has more market power.

### SECURITY SCREENING: ROADKILL & EXCELLENT COMPANIES
We utilize two different type of quantitative screens to look for new investment ideas:
- **Roadkill with a ****P****ulse**** (“****RwP****”)**:
- A significant decline in price (“roadkill”) combined with some set of financial characteristics which indicate some signs of financial life (“pulse”).
- **Excellent Company Universe** (“ECU”):
- Each analyst designs their own screen, producing a list of 200-300 companies with the “best” characteristics (“excellent companies”).  Each analyst gets to define their own characteristics and can change their criteria for an excellent company any time.  Valuation is not required as a factor but may be included.  Each analyst must be able to explain the basis for their criteria.

These two different screening modes are deliberately intended to look at different sets of companies.  The Roadkill screens focus a portion of our attention on the stocks which the market is currently punishing the most.  We will normally be buying companies that would be showing up on a Roadkill screen.  But stocks with significant recent price declines always have problems – either problems that caused the decline or problems that the stock decline caused.  As analysts and portfolio managers, it is important that we don’t spend too much of our time looking at problems.  If you spend too much time studying problem companies you begin to think problems are normal and forget what a normal company looks like, much less how an excellent company works.

The purpose of the Excellent Company Universe is to allocate time and attention to studying currently successful companies – even if the valuation isn’t attractive.  As analysts we are students of excellence in business.  Our investment approach is to seek businesses with the seeds of excellence or signs of it, but at a time when the market is focused on their problems.  But we can’t recognize these signs of excellence if we aren’t studying it, and if we don’t have an ongoing discussion about what makes an excellent business in different industries.

## FOCUS & FIT
***Our portfolio management discipline exclude******s****** certain categories of “profitable” investment ideas.  No portfolio includes all the “good” ideas. ***

*** ***In the Merchant Adventure Fund, we will miss the following:
- Short-term merger arbitrage opportunities.
- Nifty biotech ideas that are only pursuing modest goals.
- Cyclical companies of all types.
- Asset plays based on long-tail residual cash flows.
- Comebacks of overleveraged companies.
- Already massive companies with scale continuing.
- Private investment opportunities internationally.
- Private investment opportunities where we can’t learn directly from the founders.
- Public investment opportunities in focused on markets lacking domestic scale or population growth.
- Publicly traded opportunities in administratively complex markets.
- Opportunities where we can’t think of ourselves as owners and make sense of management plans.
- Etc.

The only way to be able to be structured, systematic and build expertise is through focus.  The flip side of focus is that we will miss opportunities – what statistics refers to as Type 2 errors, or “false negatives.”  In statistics, there is generally a trade-off between Type 1 errors (false positive) and Type 2 errors (false negatives).  If we want to reduce the number of false positives (in our case buying a stock that goes down) then we have to accept an increase in false negatives (or rejecting an investment that would have made money).   This is useful for analysts to understand because the natural tendency for an analyst is to view success as getting their name in the portfolio, whereas the portfolio management discipline is designed to minimize false positives which means the portfolio manager will reject many profitable ideas (false negatives) and this isn’t a bug or problem with the process – it is a feature of the process.  You can’t reduce false positives without increasing false negatives.

***In your research, you****** will ******find****** a****** constant****** stream of “probably profitable” or ******“obviously awesome” investment opportunities****** -- b******ut the portfolio only hold******s****** a few dozen positions, and most ideas won’t fit for our ******portfolio discipline***.

The brutal reality is that the portfolio averages around one new position and one exited position a month.  Do the math. With five analysts on the team then each analyst should average around 2 new names in the portfolio a year, and that means a year could pass where an analyst wouldn’t add any companies to the portfolio – and this would be a sign of doing good work.  New ideas do not equal good work – it is often the opposite.  Too many new ideas indicate the absence of a depth of understanding and an absence of work.

The inevitable and appropriate fate of most new ideas is to be shot down.  That is fine.  The point of the process isn’t the ideas, but the lessons learned from constantly scanning the business landscape for disasters, opportunities, emerging themes and excellence.
***The ultimate focus of studying business is to learn to recognize excellence.***

# COMPANY ANALYSIS
Plenty of ink has been spilled explaining how to analyze companies.  The fact of the matter is that each company is idiosyncratic – or its success is likely to be idiosyncratic.  Company analysis is a sort of reverse Anna Karenina problem: “Unsuccessful companies are all alike; every successful company is successful in its own way.”
Therefore, the task of analyzing a company is not to find the ways in which it fits some specific form, but the process of making sure that it functions and then understanding what elements could “make it successful in its own way” – in a way that it different.
A more humorous way to look at the company analysis problem and the search for exceptional businesses which could be long term winners comes from This is Spinal Tap, “these go to eleven.”   So you are looking for that company where it can go to 10, but then when it needs something a little more, it can go to 11.
Note, with these statements I am trying to fit a form to what an exceptional company looks like – but the key is really that an exceptional company in a specific business will be the only one of its kind.  I use these examples as illustrations, but that is all.  *Analyzing an exceptional company **the a** process of getting to know and understand the company by studying it over time.  It doesn’t fit a** single** form**; it** is best described as an adventure.*
## WHERE TO START
How the company sees themselves…. Company filings and reports and presentations – not sell side or media reports.
## PUTTING IT DOWN & PICKING IT BACK UP
Most companies won’t make sense the first time you look at them, and when you get to something that doesn’t make sense – then you put it down.  Being able to spend some time on a company and learn a little bit and then move on is its own skill.
But just because we have looked at something before and passed on investing doesn’t mean we have rejected it permanently.  If a company name keeps coming up or you keep coming across information on a company – you want to pick it back up and take another look and learn a little more.
This cycle of picking up and putting back down and picking up again can go on every few months for years in some cases.  This is an excellent way to learn about a company in the long-term.
## SEEING WHAT ISN’T THERE YET
Ultimately, when it is time for us to think about building a position is when we begin to be able to see what isn’t on the page yet or what hasn’t happened yet.  Understanding an existing business is simple – but to be able to see the potential of a management team and the business they are running takes focus and imagination.
## GETTING TO THE POINT
Pulling together the investment thesis. (need to write this – but its just something you learn by doing… need to model how to do it more.)  Why is it a value gorilla?

# ALLOCATING YOUR TIME
The most valuable resource we all have is our time.  In terms of making positive statements on how to allocate time in research – well that’s impossible because if you are really “doing” research, you are exploring something new – which requires focus and imagination as we have discussed.  The process can’t be precisely defined – but the requirements for useful outputs can.
By focusing on what the endpoints need to be, we can establish some simple questions/rules/principles of when research is a waste of time – shown in the graphic below which originally came from the Dodge & Cox analyst handbook circa 1992.

Note how the list isn’t about “doing” research. It’s defining the characteristics of a “merely adequate” research product.  This principle of maintaining a focus on achieving an adequate result should remind you of the May Sarton quote in the Prologue.

# THINKING LIKE AN OWNER
Owning shares of a company is owning part of the company.  That is what the shares are: ownership interests.  So, the logic of it should be clear to everyone. An owner of a business should think like an owner.  And the relevant mindset in analyzing whether to become an owner of a business is to think like an owner of that business.
This has a few simple but often ignored implications.  An owner probably isn’t worried about technical trading patterns.  An owner is worried about growth and profitability.  We think like owners and focus on growth and profitability – not technical trading patterns or any of the hundreds of other distractions the market provides.

## IMAGINE YOU ARE RUNNING THE BUSINESS
A simple unstructured exercise is to imagine that you were running the business you are studying or analyzing.  You will find that you ask questions differently in this mode and it is often useful in highlighting gaps in our knowledge or understanding of either how businesses work or elements of a specific business.
Lessons to be learned from this exercise:
- If you really understand a business you will find that you understand why management is doing things without needing to read their new releases on it or talk with them about it… If you need to talk with management to understand why they did something you don’t understand the business.
- If you can’t imagine running the business at all – either because something seems insane or doesn’t make sense to you in some way – exactly what doesn’t make sense or why you wouldn’t want to be running the business is the analytical information we are looking for.
One of the most useful insights might be the simple fact that you wouldn’t want to run a business because of legal or ethical or personal safety concerns you would feel if you were running that business.  That feeling is worth doing some research on, because if those concerns aren’t just the result of an overactive imagination, they represent material investment risks that should be part of your analysis.

## OWNER OPERATORS
The classic analysis of why owner operators can do better has to do with what is referred to as “agency problems.” Agency problems come from the misalignment of interests – where management may be running the business for their interests based on their incentives and those interests and incentives may not be in the best interests of shareholders, or the long-term business.
Because no one has a time machine – debating agency issues can be an analytical morass. So instead, we tend to look at the incentives and culture of an organization to try to understand why the organization works or doesn’t work.
In the background of all of this is another agency problem though – and that is on our side as investors.  Are we really taking a view that is aligned with the long-term best interests of the company?
And the only way to answer that question is to go back and imagine you are running the business.  Imagining we own the business and are running it is not only a key to testing our knowledge of a business – it also is a useful trick for getting around the agency issues in our organization and processes.

# INVESTMENT THESIS
The investment thesis is the synthesis of the company research process.  The thesis statement is deceptively simple and focuses us on the key risks and opportunities for the company in one to three short paragraphs.
The investment thesis offers a qualitative assessment of the business franchise and management quality, and a quantitative assessment of expected profitability and growth relative to current valuation.  A well-structured investment thesis will only need to have the quantitative assessment updated periodically, while the qualitative assessment could remain accurate and relevant for years.  The idea that the qualitative assessment of the business and management in an investment thesis should be durable and timeless is important to keep in mind. If a thesis included statements that won’t be relevant or true in 3 years, you aren’t done yet.  You may have an important observation, but it isn’t part of the investment thesis.
Note: It is tempting to include add and trim prices as well as expected events in the investment thesis – but these elements are not part of the investment thesis.  The add/trim price lives in the portfolio management process and expected events are part of the scenario process – and these processes need to be where those data items are managed/updated.

## VALUE GORILLAS
Our elevator pitch of each investment should be why each holding is a value gorilla. What is the economic opportunity in front of it and what are they doing to exploit this opportunity? Why is it likely to succeed while the market is pricing the stock as if they will not succeed?  That's it.
Value Gorilla’s are characterized by the following traits:
1. The unlocking of a large amount of value through applying a combination of multiple old known technology to a choke point in a key value chain (Gorilla large, inspired by the old Geoffrey Moore book -https://www.harpercollins.com/products/the-gorilla-game-revised-edition-geoffrey-a-moore ).
2. Which is obvious, although not necessarily believable (Gorilla in the picture but did you notice it - http://theinvisiblegorilla.com/gorilla_experiment.html).
We can then look for companies which are working on these opportunities leveraging existing businesses and clients, and whose businesses trade as though the opportunities will not be realized (invisible gorilla / value stock / free real options).
The investment thesis for companies in the fund can be thought of as a brief explanation of what makes the business a value gorilla, with a minimal amount of structure to it – so that we make sure we touch on the key elements of business, management, and valuation.

## SENTIEO THESIS TEMPLATE
The investment thesis should be kept update in the thesis template or thesis note for each company in Sentieo.  The thesis note is made up of two parts: (1) the thesis statement, (2) a brief section of essential summary statistics.

## ESSENTIAL STATISTICS
For our decision process and investment framework, the essential statistics are:
- Current Price
- Shares outstanding
- Market Cap
- Total Debt
- Shareholder’s Equity*
- Cash
- Sales*
- Gross Margin
- Net Margin
- Price to Sales*
- Price to Book*
- Dividend Yield
- Expected Growth FY0 to FY2: Book and Sales
**Question: Should we include Expected Book Growth and Expected Sales Growth calculated based on FY2 consensus analyst estimates compared to the most recent year end (so a **2 year** compound near term growth rate) and then use FY2 Expected Sales and FY2 Expected Book in the valuation multiples?  This implicitly creates a sort of 2 stage financial modelling structure where we “outsource” the **near term** modelling to the sell-side analysts who are focused on that, and then we use the estimate from the end of the time horizon they are focused on as the base point for anchoring our longer-term view…*

# FINANCIAL SIGNPOSTS
One can easily get lost in all the financial signposts, ratios and indicators that different practitioners promote.
Our goal is to try to boil all the complexity down to simple frameworks.  This doesn’t get rid of the complexity – but it offers us a way to contain it, the same way that a line item in an accounting statement contains a mess of journal entries but can be understood without needing to worry about the journal entries.
In our valuation work, everything rolls up to measures of profitability and growth.  This brief note is intended to introduce a few financial statement indicators of market power and management skill.  The idea being that without market power and management skill it is difficult to have much confidence in the ability of a business to deliver financial performance over time.  So finding indicators of market power and management skill in a companies financial statements is a useful check or confirmation of a company’s capability of performing.
## MARKET POWER
There are two classic signs of market power that are part of every set of financial statements:
- *Gross Margin Stability*: a company which can maintain a stability of gross margins, or is able to hold steady the mark-up they get on a product over their cost to make the product (cost of good sold) displays a combination of discipline and market power.  Long-term market power is stability through an economic cycle or a period including a strong economy and a weak economy.  Market power is not the ability to increase gross margins, since that’s something customers will notice and won’t appreciate in the long run.
- *Negative Working Capital**:* If a company can require its customers to pay it for goods quickly while it can also negotiate with suppliers to give it a long time to pay, the company has market power.  Quantitatively this shows up as days payables outstanding minus days sales outstanding being a large positive number.
Market power is a sign of skill but it is more complicated than just management skill, because in the case of market power we are observing a more complex phenomenon or set of circumstances that enables a business to be able to charge and hold steady there mark-up on their product through economic cycles, or where their customers are happy to pay quickly and their suppliers will settle for being paid later,  Power is this ability to dictate price and to dictate payment.
There are other factors that are arguably signs of market power – but these two are the main ones.  The only tricky factor is that everyone knows these metrics are being watched, so you also must learn to look for how companies play games.  That will take you into the accounting literature.

## MANAGEMENT SKILL
The way I quantify management skill is similar to the framework of Raef Raesendes and Dan Obrycki of the Applied Finance Group (“AFG”).  A lot of the basic modelling assumptions and techniques in AFG’s Economic Margin Framework are just best practices accounting adjustments to get at the underlying economics of the business.  Then they add some other fancy stuff that I don’t agree with – but all the basics are sound.
### LONG-TERM CAPITAL ALLOCATION
The concept that I think I got from them was the idea that the CEO’s most important job is long-term capital allocation.  So you can score the CEO, or come up with a management score, based on long-term capital allocation.  And the CEO’s job in allocating capital is basically to drain capital from underperforming businesses and allocate additional capital to outperforming businesses.  So the CEO is doing a good job if they are decreasing capital and returns are steady or improving, or if they are increasing capital and returns are improving.  The no-no is increasing capital and decreasing returns.
Notice I haven’t specified the time horizon over which to measure change in capital and change in returns on capital?  Yeah – that’s the difficult part with this measure.  So it’s not quite as simple as just running a financial model of a point in time, but you can graph the returns on capital and changes in invested capital over time and when you know a business you can walk through the story it tells and that understanding is a useful perspective on how the CEO has performed in allocating long-term capital.
To quickly step back, the importance of reinvestment of profits and long-term capital allocation is easy to forget.  To make it simple, if a company has a return on equity of 20%, that means that in the next five years the CEO will allocate an amount of capital to projects in excess of the existing equity capital base of the company.  So as long-term investors, the way that capital is allocated will be one of the key drivers of what the business is five years from now.  And how profits are reinvested and long-term capital allocated is one of the CEO’s key responsibilities.
As analysts, we might not be able to have a precise quantitative scorecard for the CEO, but we can get an EKG of sorts by looking at a long time series of relevant data.

### SHORT-TERM CAPITAL ALLOCATION
While the CEO is focused on the long-term capital cycle, the CFO’s daily job includes the way the company breathes cash in and out of its accounts.  The CFO’s scorecard is the short-term or the working capital cycle.
Now, negative working capital was also a sign of market power – but it is important to say that negative working capital isn’t a positive sign of CFO skill.  The structure or amount of working capital that a business may need to run efficiently isn’t at the discretion of the CFO.  The CFO’s job is to run the business he is part of as efficiently as possible, The CFO scorecard is based on the stability or consistent incremental improvement of the working capital accounts over time.  An exceptional CFO will maintain steady working capital accounts through different economic conditions, and you will see signs of incremental efficiencies in some of the numbers.  But the key is the ability to maintain consistency.
The relationship of the working capital accounts is best visualized as a time series graph converting each account to “days balances” so the that graph is showing you a picture of the timing of how cash goes through the working capital accounts: payables, inventory, receivables, cash.

## FUTURE WORK
We have old screens and templates for looking at these items, but a good task for an intern or junior analyst would be to start with a blank sheet of paper and think about how they would create tools to visualize or evaluate these and other financial signposts.
The process of building visualizations for financial ratios and metrics over time is a wonderful way to begin to link the lines in a chart with events or decisions driving a business.

# TURD BLOSSOMS
While George W. Bush’s term of endearment for his chief of staff Karl Rove is emotionally loaded for some or perhaps a little off color for others – the evocative phrase has a useful meaning to us.
A turd blossom is literally a flower that grows from a pile of manure.  Rhetorically, it echoes Reagan’s famous story of seeing a pile of manure and looking for the pony – or the more sublime observations that “out of the mud grows the lotus.”  The lotus allusion is more polite, but it’s also in the title of Goldie Hawn’s autobiography – so we are sticking with the Texas derivation instead of either California versions of it.
Symbolically, the turd blossom is the good the comes after – after the deluge, if one is in a more biblical mood. The relevance of the turd blossom to us is, in its way like the rainbow to Noah.  But the sign of better days for us is some company event, not a bending of light.

## Technical Meaning in Company Research
We buy companies which we think are long term winners when the market thinks they aren’t.
We are usually buying when a company has real problems – those problems are why the market has low expectations for the stock.  In colloquial terms, the stock is priced like a steaming turd, and like it’s future is just to get cold and being a cold turd is the only thing worse than a warm turd.
As analysts – the question at that point is whether the turd will blossom?  What signs should we watch for and when and why would we give up hope for the turd blossom?
The early shoots of the turd blossom are the signs of improvement – of mediocre corporate performance being converted into something good, which could be a step to building an exceptional company in the long-term or at least a period of outperformance.
***In the terms of this note – ******what we do is…  invest****** in human progress by collecting ******“things”****** likely to blossom.  ***

## Usefulness of Humor
It’s not fun and takes a certain temperament to see the potential in a messy or bad situation or underperforming company – just as it is an odd thing to look at a turd and watch for it to blossom.  To continue mixing metaphors, it is like whistling through the graveyard and across the railroad tracks.
Taking risk is part of investing – but taking risk is stressful and investors aren’t Vulcans.  We feel the stress and risk and letting if off with humor is the best way to manage the stress,  You want to recognize the stress, and then laugh at it and then step back and use the perspective laughter yields to analyze what was bothering you.
Turd blossoms are funny by nature – unexpected and foul and sublime in way too.  Thinking of an underperforming company as a potential turd blossom offers a good way to acknowledge the problems while challenging everyone to look at the problems as “fertilizer” for what’s next.
***And what’s next is always what we need to be focused on, even if that starts with imagining a steaming pile….  ***
***All true beginnings aren’t just hard, they are also humble.***

# [DRAFT] VALUE EXPECTATIONS
**<< this will get directly specified in the work we are doing with Ivan and James.>>**
Value driver – Growth – Profitability - Adjustments
The ability to build a full model of the projected financial statements of a company is a useful skill to have.  There is no better way to get an understanding of how financial statements are linked together and the way money flows through the different financial statements.  That said – once one has built a number of these models, you can’t help but notice that the value of the model is in this understanding of the flows and linkages – because the models inevitably fail to predict the future financials.  The error rate in the model ends up being proportional to its level of precision.
In forecasting financial expectations our goal is to trade off precision for accuracy. To do this we make a series of simplifying assumptions.  Each assumption may seem simple and has the effect of simplifying our analysis and math – but there is solid fundamental basis for each simplifying assumption and none of these things are actually shortcuts – they are mor like tunnels burrowed through rock.
The simplifying assumptions are:
- Stage 1 - One-Two Years Out: The Sell side Consensus is probably actually fairly accurate for the next 12-18 months, or we aren’t likely to consistently pay more attention to the near future wiggles and wobbles than the street analysts – so the most efficient way to financially model the near future is to use the consensus numbers.
- Stage 2 – Three – Five Years Out: There may be a midterm growth phase or adjustment phase – a period of normalization between the near-term stuff in the analysts model and the business settling into what we think is a likely long term growth and profitability range.
- Stage 3: Five Years out – Business has stabilized or we just assume it will have to be able to model it – at a multiple which reflects our expectation of the long-term growth and profitability of the business.
- We view growth and profitability as being driven either by sales or book/capital.  We identify the value driver for the business as either sales or book and then quantify the long term growth as growth in sales or growth in book and long term profitability as net margins or ROE.
- Using a 15% Target rate of return for all investments, we get a target terminal multiple 5 years out of price to book or price to sales.
- We allow for adjustments, which usually would involve balance sheet adjustments -- adjusting for cash, stock issuance/buybacks, mergers or spin-offs or debt repayment.
- We take the value driver at the end of Stage 1, with the analysts concerns about the near term issues baked in, then we make our adjustments, grow or shrink it in an intermediate period (stage 2) and show our math.
- In its simplest form, the difference between the multiple today and the long-term multiple plus growth in the next 5 years is your return on the investment over 5 years.  The return is expected growth plus multiple expansion.

# [DRAFT] Scenario Process & Tools
**<< also being defined in the application we are working on with Ivan & James.>>**
Our scenario process helps us construct detailed event-based expectations for our companies and the markets they operate in.  From an academic perspective, we build on the work of Tetlock in Superforecasting.  The process is designed as a disciplined way to prepare for the future and how the near future will differ from the recent past.  The key analytical errors we are trying to adjust for are expressed by Peter Schwartz in his call for imagination and analytical realism.  We must not assume that things will not change and we must be skeptical of boosters breathlessly overstating changes.
Operationally, our scenario process focuses on constructing sets of key future events, structured as yes or no questions (binomial outcomes) over fixed time horizons in different contexts.  Mario Orellana and John Magruder are working on constructing an Observable Notebook building off of a Tidy Tree Conditional Probability Notebook created by another Observable user.
Our template will be set up to take as input the following:
- Type of Scenario
- Subject
- Events
- Order or Path Dependence
- Future Considerations
The subject is the company or domain/issue which the events in the scenario apply to.  The events should be a list of questions framed to fit the type of analysis and including a probability estimate.  Functionally, an event is composed of (1) a name, (2) a  yes or no question with includes a specific date / deadline, (3) if the event is dependent on another event, path dependence / order, (4) our probability estimate of the event, (5) basis for the probability estimate specifically in terms of either technical feasibility or scaling/distribution issues.
The four types of scenarios:
- Company Update (Quarterly or other periodic updates)
- Company Analysis (Investment Thesis)
- Emerging Economic Potential (Short-term Disruption/Shift)
- Economic Potential Analysis (Long-term Economic Drivers)
In the case of the first and second types of scenarios, the subject is a company – but the difference between the types is the time horizon under consideration.  In the third and fourth type of scenario, the subject could be a country or an industry or the path of a specific technology and the difference is, time horizon.  Each type of scenario forces us think expected events in different contexts at different time horizons.  We are not predicting the future – but if we can more quickly see what has happened and rapidly discard things that are not possible – well, that’s how one gets an analytical advantage that isn’t based on luck.
The three time horizons for scenarios are:
- Tactical (12 to 18 months),
- Strategic (3-5 years),
- Long-term (7-10 years).
The purpose of the tactical time horizon is to take the key challenges & opportunities we know a company is facing in the near term and frame them in terms of questions for us to watch.  The key here isn’t really to get the probabilities right – strangely enough getting the probabilities right only begins to matter as we refine this process over time. The key here is to make sure we can articulate the key opportunities and challenges each of our companies faces in the next 12-18 months and have a framework for tracking their progress.  Additionally – once we have events for all the companies, we can also look at the portfolio in terms of weights that are dependent on future events.  So the events could function as a forward looking basis for thinking about risks in the portfolio.
The strategic time horizon could be applied to a major economic shift which is under way and which we want to try to detail out more precisely or it would also apply to the construction of the investment thesis for a company.  A specific example of a near term economic potential analysis which I think we should put together is the emergence of nuclear power.  This is already happening because of Russia’s recent actions and the climate beliefs of the millennials and gen-z.  So what events should we be watching for as this gets started? In terms of the strategic company analysis – we should be able to articulate the events which would need to happen for our underlying investment thesis on a company to hold true.  So the strategic company analysis should include a set of events pertaining to management, business franchise / financials and valuation which would map to our investment thesis for the company.
The longer-term time horizon only applies to long-term economic potential and general domains of economic opportunity.  There is no real granularity to this sort of analysis – but a well-constructed long-term analysis of economic potential should inform what issues we need to dive into and understand in more detail and so while this is less granular and longer term, it is not disconnected from the other time horizons.  A long-term view must be consistent with the shorter-term views.
The table below summarizes types of scenarios, subjects of each analysis and time horizons involved.

|  | Type of Scenario |
| --- | --- |
|  | Company Update | Company Investment Thesis | Emerging Economic Potential | Economic Potential Analysis |
|  | *Subject* | *Company* | *Company* | *N**ot a Company* | *Not** a Company* |
| Time Horizon | Tactical (12 – 18 mths) | **#** |  |  |  |
|  | Strategic (3-5 yrs) |  | **#** | **#** |  |
|  | Long-term (7-10 yrs) |  |  |  | **#** |

The output of the scenario process is a set of key questions regarding the future of the subject, where the questions are structured around a subject, an event, a time horizon and a probability.  In terms of estimating probabilities, we will need to use common principles with a shared basis so that we can have intelligent discussions about the probabilities. Ultimately, the probabilities are more useful for helping us rank order the likelihood of a series of events than they will be for precisely knowing in any fashion what will happen.  There are three general levels of certainty:
- Proximate: It has already started to happen in some way, but could stop (67% or higher)
- Probable: all the elements exist for it to happen (33% to 66%)
- Possible: could happen but unlikely (less than 32%).
Thinking about the future is difficult.  Most scenario planning processes are designed to drive strategic decisions about how businesses plan to create change or evolve – scenario planning is designed for actors in the real economy.  But as investors we are not actors or managers, but observers like Drucker’s bystander.  As investors, we can use the overall scenario planning frameworks to mentally prepare ourselves, and structure our own expectations – allowing us to see changes in the economy as they are happening.  Investing is a strange game.  There is a stability to it because individual people, humans, don’t really change – but our technologies, societies and cultures are constantly evolving.  What I am trying to introduce as a structured part of our investment process is the use of scenario thinking to imagine or construct sets of key future events for us to watch for.  These events should be steps in the evolution of business or technology or society from what it is now to something new, and a useful tool in observing progress.

# PORTFOLIO GUIDELINES & TRADING
No one step in the portfolio guideline process is complicated.  Each step, each piece is simple.  But the guideline process brings together company research, portfolio management and trading.   So, while each piece is simple, the ways in which all the pieces interact can be complex,
## WHAT ARE GUIDELINES?
The guideline for a company is a static target weight for the company in the portfolio.  The guidelines can add up to 100 or less.  Frequently, the guideline weights in stocks will only add up to 96% to 99%.
Because the guideline is a static weight and the actual weights of companies in the portfolio constantly shifts as prices change, stocks which are declining in price or lagging the market will decline in weight and tend to fall behind their guidelines and stocks which increase in price or outperform tend to rise above their guidelines.
Part of the portfolio review process is to reset guidelines where appropriate to match lower or higher weights if after a shift in price we decide not to buy or sell a position to guideline.  In this way, the static guideline process is a way of deliberately setting & resetting the weights in the portfolio and creates active decision and discussion points for the portfolio management team.
## SETTING GUIDELINES
Active portfolio management breaks down to two things: getting the stocks right and getting the weights right.  The decision isn’t just to buy or sell, but also how much, when and at what price?  Our process of working through the guidelines is designed to keep us focused on getting the stocks right, and the weights right – how much, when and at what price.
***The guidelines for the Merchant Adventure Fund are reviewed during our weekly investment team meeting.******  All team members can recommend changes either at the meeting or during the week.****** The weekly investment team meeting is the only time guidelines are changed******, so recommended changes can be que******ued up for the meeting or presented at the meeting.  Research meetings focused on company updates happen more frequently during the week, so the research recommendation from an analysts would be tracked as a recommended change to be reviewed at the next investment team meeting.***
When we are reviewing company weights in the portfolio, we have a simple rule of thumb explicitly and deliberately linking the investment thesis which we maintain as part of our research process with the “default” or “natural” weight for a company in the portfolio.  The investment thesis has three parts: business franchise, management quality, and valuation.  Confidence in each element of the thesis is worth 1% in guideline: powerful business model and competitive position  (+1%), an exceptional inspirational management team (+1%), and a low valuation reflecting that very little value exists (+1%).  In its simplest form, if we feel confident in all three parts of the thesis for a company, it’s a 3% position.  Confident in one of the three – that’s a 1% position.  Confident to the level of feeling certain and we’ve been following or owned the company for years – well the guideline can go higher than 3%.
This linkage of the guideline to the investment thesis naturally translates shifts in an investor thesis into the guideline discussion.  Does the weight match our outlook and confidence in the three elements of the investment thesis? Based on that there is a default weight of 1% or 2% or 3% and then the question is if that is the right weight for the portfolio, or if there are other considerations that would cause us to hold more or less of it?
With a 20-40 stock portfolio, our average position size will always be above 2%.  The maximum guideline weight for a position is 10%.
Beyond the simple logical structure to link the investment thesis to the guideline weight, which serves as a useful long-term reference point – there will be shorter-term tactical considerations that come into play in adjusting guidelines.  Part of the guideline process is also making “floating changes” to the guidelines when a stock may have moved up or down, but we are waiting for some additional information or news before buying or selling.  This is a functional accommodation to the realities of managing a portfolio – the goal is to be trading the portfolio to the guideline weights and so we need to take into account percentages of the portfolio which we aren’t using because we aren’t buying right now or which are being used because we aren’t selling.
## BUYING OR SELLING TO GUIDELINES
***Whenever we are buying or selling shares, we refer to it as buying or selling to ******guideline***.  That’s just how it works. The guideline in a stock has to be above or below the current weight of the stock in the portfolio for us to trade, but this is only one of three conditions or triggers that need to be in place for us to buy or sell.
The other two conditions are:
- The Price is Right:  Above the Trim Price for Selling / Below the Add Price for Buying
- More than a Bite:    To get to guideline we need to buy or sell more than 0.25%
When all three conditions are met, we may buy or sell a position to guideline.  The process of buying or selling a position to guideline and how that is broken into steps or timed is at the discretion of the trader.

## SETTING THE ADD AND TRIM PRICES
Add and trim prices start out as buy and sell target prices set in the original security recommendation.  But once a position has been established these prices can and will be adjusted to help manage the position in a disciplined way.
Add and trim prices were created as an tool for averaging into a stock as it declines in price or averaging out of a position as it appreciates.  Philosophical, the add and trim price reflect the fact that we aren’t trying to predict the perfect buy or sell price.  We are pragmatists. We have developed a process over time that naturally encourages us to average our costs on a holding down as prices decline and to average our realizations up as it appreciates.
The process is simple.  Let’s assume that a stock is appreciating and rises above it’s trim price, and that as a result of the appreciation the weight of the holding in the portfolio is 1% over its guideline.  Now the investment team has a two decision to make:
- Getting an update from the analyst asking if the trim price was still “right”.
- Deciding if the Guideline should changed or “floated” to the position.
If the trim price is raised or the guideline is raised such that the deviation from the guideline is less than 0.25% - then the team has decided not to trade.  But if the trim price isn’t changed and the guideline isn’t floated, or maybe the team just increases the guideline 0.5%. then part of the position can be sold. The team is effectively deciding to trim the position but doing it indirectly by checking elements of a process.
For our example here – let’s assume we trim the holding.  Once the trades are made bringing the holding to the guideline, the trader will increase the trim price between 15% to 30%, such that if nothing else changed but the price the stock would be more than 0.25% above guideline if it were to appreciate to the updated trim price.  This process of updating the trim price upward mechanically after a round of trades is a mechanical ratchet that sets the default behavior to averaging out of an appreciating position.
The same thing is done but “in reverse” for stocks where we are buying because the price has declined.  The ratchet there works to force us to talk about averaging down on positions or to admit that we got it wrong and need to exit the position.
## INTERNAL DATA FIELDS
In making estimates and creating a data frame for this process, we track a set of our own data items.
- Lead Analyst
- Shares held
- Guideline
- Recommended Changes
- Add Price
- Trim Price
- Value Driver
- Profitability
- Growth
- Target Multiple
- Notes

## FUTURE WORK
Currently the guidelines and associated information as stored in a spreadsheet which works – but can be improved on.  We should be looking at better ways to do this – ideally through a secure web interface.
The quick and dirty valuation framework in the spreadsheet: value driver, profitability, growth and resulting target multiple --  needs to be written up and implemented in a way that these fields link to the investment thesis for a company.
The notes field is used for a variety of different things – from reminders for dates for wash sales to expected next events to next research tasks.  The notes field should be written up and in the process we should link that to the actual data it contains and this process will also highlight additional functionality we may want in the next iteration of the guidelines “interface”…
There is always room to learn and improve.

# Private Company Investments
In the summer of 2022, Merchant Adventure made its first private company investment in Reduct Inc. The founders of Reduct, Robert and Prabhas, are promising young leaders in programming and design.  Reduct is working on re-imagining how people process and curate video – which is economically significant.  All the normal analytical details lined up.  But with smaller companies the real risks come from a whole host of factors which cannot be anticipated at this early stage.  In Kauffman’s terms, the early-stage private company is exploring the adjacent possible and functionally testing a hypothesis that is un-pre-statable.
Although the goal is for private investments to be profitable, the prospective investment return is a variable that we cannot control, and our standard public market risk control tools: low valuations and long time horizons, don’t work. We are also not investing in enough promising early-stage companies for the functionally random risks of scaling to be averaged out through many positions, as has become the norm at successful venture investment firms.
***The real return which Merchant Adventure can be sure to earn from investing in private companies is to gain domain knowledge on innovations in economically significant areas.   Our private investment discipline and differentiation is our focus on learning.  ****We invest in companies advancing human progress, and our primary focus is on public companies.  We invest in private companies to help understand emerging threats and opportunities to public companies.*  Given time constraints and limits to the amount of the portfolio invested in private companies, we will invest in no more than 2 new private companies per calendar year.  Most of our activities in the private company space over time will be follow-on deals with existing investments and learning from those management teams.
Therefore, the key criteria for Merchant Adventure in reviewing private investments are:
- DOMAIN: Company pursuing a large target market in a domain of interest to Merchant.
- OVERLAP: No other existing private company investments with overlapping markets.
- MODEL: Target company has modelled the problem in their own way that offers new insight (like seeing Gravity and realizing it holds up the moon and pulls down the rain, etc.).  The *model* creates new tools to add to a society’s or an economy’s *arsenal* for solving a socially and economically important issue.
- DEPTH: Management team has a depth of knowledge and experience in the area – should have been working on problems in the domain for at least a decade such that we can check references informally and also review past work in academic journals, GitHub or elsewhere.
- ALLOCATION: We can see allocating $1MM or more to the investment over time and that capital can be deployed in a way that would be meaningful to the target company.  We are not trying to compete to write them checks. We look to allocate our capital in ways that maximize our impact and learning.
- RECIPROCATION: Management needs to be natural reciprocators. Reciprocation is the flywheel for human relationships and creates the back and forth where learning takes place.  It is easy and natural to learn from and collaborate with reciprocators.
- CAPACITY: Have we already done 2 new company deals in the last 12 months or calendar year? Who will be committed to advising the company and communicating the lessons learned?
- ALTERNATIVES: All the alternatives compete for the fund’s capital and our time.  Is adding a new company “better” than adding to a position in an existing private company or public company?  Exactly how do we think it’s better?

# ROLE OF MACROECONOMICS & CURRENCIES

Our investment approach focuses on three key factors: businesses, management, and valuation.  Macroeconomics and currencies are not independent factors in our framework.  This doesn’t mean that we ignore macroeconomics and currencies – it is just that to treat them as analytical entities on their own is a form of double counting. Let me explain with a few simple questions focused on the areas of our analytical focus.
## BUSINESS
When we analyze a business, how do we come up with expected growth rates and margins?  We will be factoring in macroeconomic expectations there because the company will be taking those conditions into account in their plans.  The company will also consider expected currency issues and may or may not hedge.  Additionally, we will likely adjust our expectations based on non-consensus macro outlooks for primary markets for the company.  Macroeconomic expectations are embedded in our assessment of the business.
## MANAGEMENT
Macroeconomic conditions are never as “macro” as they seem to be.  The devil is always in the details and economies are incomprehensibly complex.  The role of management is to understand the details of the specific positioning, assets, suppliers, markets, and so on – which their company must negotiate, navigate and profit from.  In our assessment of management, we either believe they have the skills to navigate those details, or we should not be investing in the company.  Part of our process is to assess management’s ability to adapt and profit from macro-economic shocks.  So we expect our companies to act based on changing macro-economics on their own.  Companies are not static entities.  For us to act and adapt when the companies are acting, when part of out initial thesis on management should have been that they have better information and have an impressive capability to make decisions and drive execution – well it seems like our action might be redundant or counterproductive.
## VALUATION
Finally, doesn’t most of the market seem to act based on perceived macro-economic information like inflation or rising rates?  This indisputable fact has two important implications: (1) often the macro-economic information is already in the price, while (2) management teams reactions and adaptations are not in the price.  Thinking through these simple ideas on how macro-economic “information” impacts the market is useful.  The profit is always in focusing on what’s not in the price – and that is the company specifics.  That happens to be what our process is designed to focus on.

That’s the basic story.  But the basic story always leaves something out.  You may remember the September 2019 letter:
*Shakespeare was a master of **ellipsis**, of leaving things out**..**.*
Bloom, Harold. Cleopatra: I Am Fire and Air

## THE ELLIPSIS
We are not masters of ellipsis, and the goal of defining these processes is not to leave anything out – but that is an impossible goal.  When we leave things out – it is an error of omission.  Errors of omission are the most dangerous.
In the case of macro-economics and currencies, what this means is that we don’t ignore macro-economics. That would be a serious error of omission.  Many investors confidently assert they ignore macro when what they actually do is delegate responsibility to adapting to macro conditions to company management.  We want to be explicit about that.
The ellipsis, or analytical error, in macro analysis is to fail to explicitly assess how management adapts to different expected and unexpected changes in their markets – of which currency shifts and macro-economic shocks and shifts are categories of changes which management must be prepared to adapt to.
I highlight this omission because it is an error I have made repeatedly in my career and something I remind myself of regularly.  It isn’t enough to find an excellent business with visionary management at a low valuation – the business and management also have to be prepared to adapt to changes in the macro conditions.

# ALIEN PHILOSOPHIES & ANTIBODIES

This one is very simple.  We must defend against foreign ways of thinking – thinking that is not defined in our framework or within our shared language.  We must defend against it like a disease and develop organizational antibodies to reject alien investment philosophies.
This isn’t because ours is the only way of making money, or because we somehow “see the truth” with some vision of “intrinsic value” or other nonsense.  It is because to achieve excellence we need to focus, using our shared imagination and shared language to do more as a team than any of us could as individuals.
There are so many alien philosophies, that it is always easier to come back to Polonious’ advice to Laertes – the part after neither lender nor borrower be: “this above all else, to thine own self be true.”  But replace self with investment philosophy, approach, or discipline.
Alien philosophies can be very successful, but this does not mean that we can succeed being someone else.  A hawk does not decide to try hunting like a cat, nor does a cat try to hunt like a hawk – cats and hawks each hunt in their own way, in the way they have adapted or evolved to hunt.  We are not macro investors; we are not short-term traders; we are not merger arbitrage players; we neither buy nor sell volatility as a thing in itself; we do not short; we do not borrow on margin; we have no use for technical analysis; the patterns we see involve profitability, growth and company financials, or they are the patterns of innovation and diffusion which haven’t diffused to valuation.
As Laertes lamented to Hamlet, “there are more things on heaven and earth than are dreamt of in your philosophie.”  And that is the point.  We are curious about all those things, but we will always be asking how to understand in our terms – in terms of business, management, and valuation, in terms of expected events and valuation expectations, in terms of gorilla sized opportunities and invisible gorilla, in terms of complex interactions and simple rules, from the owner’s perspective, in terms of human progress and the digital path we are now on.

# TOYOTA KATA
Rother, Mike. Toyota Kata: Managing People for Improvement, Adaptiveness and Superior Results

**RELEVANCE:**
*Toyota is an exceptional company and its long-term excellence in industrial processes is worth studying.  Excerpts below are from Mike Rother’s excellent and succinct book **Toyota Kata**.  As you read the passages below from Rother describing industrial process excellence, pay attention for the ways in which the simple rules in a kata are a behavioral model of the simple rules in biology or physics or elsewhere – and the value of thinking in systems, capabilities, and complexity – rather than in static states.*
*This guidebook is the beginning of Merchant Adventure defining its own set of “kata” or practices.  While I reviewed the literature on knowledge organizations and investment businesses, I believe the best guide in what we are trying to build is as a set of disciplines or practices which are “habits of mind” – like kata and implement them to build Merchant Adventure.*

If an organization wants to thrive by continually improving and evolving, then it needs systematic procedures and routines—methods—that channel our human capabilities and achieve the potential. Such routines would guide and support everyone in the organization by giving them a specific pattern for how they should go about sensing, adapting, and improving.
***Toyota has a method, or means, to do exactly that. At Toyota, improvement and adaptation are systematic and the method is a fundamental component of every task performed, not an add-on or a special initiative. Everyone at Toyota is taught to operate in this standard way, and it is applied to almost every situation. This goes well beyond just problem-solving techniques, to encompass a firm-specific behavior routine. Developing and maintaining this behavior in the organization, then, is what defines the task of management. ***
Upon closer inspection, Toyota’s way, as it is sometimes called, is characterized less by its tools or principles than by sets of procedural sequences—thinking and behavior patterns—that when repeated over and over in daily work lead to the desired outcome. These patterns are the context within which Toyota’s tools and principles are developed and function. If there is one thing to look at in trying to understand and perhaps emulate Toyota’s success, then these behavior patterns and how they are taught may well be it.
## Kata
In Japan such patterns or routines are called kata (noun). The word stems from basic forms of movement in martial arts, which are handed down from master to student over generations. Some common translations or definitions are:
- A way of doing something; a method or routine
- A pattern
- A standard form of movement
- A predefined, or choreographed, sequence of movements
- The customary procedure
- A training method or drill
… there is a further definition and translation for the word:
- A way of keeping two things in alignment or synchronization with one another
…..
## The Management Challenge
***Based on what I have been learning, the challenge we face is not to turn the heads of executives and managers toward implementing new production or management techniques or adopting new principles, but to achieving systematic continuous evolution and improvement across the organization by developing repeatedly and consistently applied behavioral routines: kata. ***Note that this challenge is significantly different than what we have been working on so far in our lean implementation efforts, and is primarily an issue of how we manage and lead people. Some adjustment in how we have been trying to adopt “lean manufacturing” will be necessary.
Before we go on I should mention that ***the idea of standardized behavioral routines often generates a prognosis that they will disable our creativity and limit our potential. What if, however, we can be even more creative, competitive, smart, out-of-the-box, and successful precisely because we have a routine that does a better job of tapping and channeling our human capabilities? A difference lies in what we define as the routine. Notably, Toyota’s improvement ******kata****** does not specify a content—it cannot—since that varies from time to time and situation to situation, but instead only the form that our thinking and behavior should take as we react to a situation. ***
Humans derive a lot of their sense of security and confidence—what psychologist Albert Bandura calls “self-efficacy,” from predictable routines: from doing things the same way again and again. However, it’s not possible for the content of what we do to stay the same, and if we try to artificially maintain it, it causes problems, because we are then adjusting to reality far too late and in a jerky manner. Any organization whose members can face unpredictable and uncertain situations (which are the norm) with confidence and effective action, because they have learned a behavioral routine for doing that, can enjoy a competitive advantage.
Toyota’s improvement kata is an excellent example of this second kind of routine. It tells us how to proceed, but not the content, and thus gives members of the organization an approach, a means, for handling an infinite variety of situations and being successful. We may be standing before a different way of operating our organizations, which can take us toward nearly any achievement we might envision.
….
## Stay Home
***One lesson implicit in this discussion is that we should not spend too much time benchmarking what others—including Toyota—are doing. You yourself are the benchmark***:
*Where are you now?  *
*Where do you want to be next?  *
*What obstacles are preventing you from getting there? *

For example, if you find that your technical support staff cannot respond quickly enough to machine problems, you might think, “I wonder how Toyota handles this?” Or you could stay home and ask, “How fast do we want our technical support to respond? What is preventing that from happening? What do we need to do to achieve the desired condition?”
***Remember, the ability of your company to be competitive and survive lies not so much in ******solutions****** themselves, but in the capability of the people in your organization to understand a situation and develop ******solutions. And you don’t have to be perfect, just ahead of your competitors in aspects of your product or service.***
***….***
## Focusing On Process Instead of Blame
Toyota’s approach of not stigmatizing failures, but instead utilizing them to learn and move forward, has an interesting effect: thinking that an abnormality or problem is neither positive nor negative shifts the focus from the individual to the process. We know that most problems are caused by the system within which people work, rather than by the individuals themselves. Therefore, Toyota maintains a no-blame focus on the process, instead of on the people around the problem. The assumptions are:
- *People are doing their best.  *
- *A problem is a system problem, not an operator problem.*
- *There is a reason for everything, and we can work together to understand the reason for a problem. *

An elegant question in this respect that I learned from Toyota is, “What is preventing the operators from working according to the standard?” I encourage you to utilize this question as you strive for a target condition, because it alters your thinking and changes where you look when a problem occurs.

***Be hard on the process, but soft on the operators.***
….
## The Five Questions
The five questions are a summary of Toyota’s approach for moving toward a target condition and are perhaps the most useful information in this book, now that you know what they mean.
They are highly effective in practice:
***1. What is the target condition? (The challenge) ***
***2. What is the actual condition now? ***
***3. What obstacles are preventing you from reaching the target condition? Which one are you addressing now? ***
***4. What is your next step? (Start of next PDCA cycle) ***
***5. When can we go and see what we have learned from taking that step? ***

The five questions come into play once you are “on the staircase,” that is, in the Plan-Do-Check-Act (“PDCA”) phase of the improvement kata, after a target condition has been established. The questions build upon one another. The better you’ve defined the target condition, the better you’ll be able to assess the current condition. The better you assess the current condition, the better you can recognize obstacles. The better you recognize obstacles, the better you can define your next step. Note that before a target condition has been established, the order of questions 1 and 2 is reversed from what is shown here.
***This sequence of five questions is a device to give you a routine and mental pattern for approaching any process or situation, and to help you learn the improvement kata. The questions distill part of the improvement ******kata****** down to a point where it becomes accessible and usable by anyone. ***They are a “minikata,” if you will, perfect for practicing. I keep the five questions in mind any time I visit a process and apply them to many other activities as well. I highly recommend that you use and internalize them.
## What Toyota Emphasizes in Problem-Solving
***Despite what the words “problem******-******solving” might lead us to think, the primary focus in problem solving at Toyota is not solutions but understanding the current situation in a work system so deeply, firsthand, that the right solution (called a countermeasure) becomes obvious and practically falls in your lap. ***Most of the effort of problem solving at Toyota is placed in grasping the situation—deeply understanding the conditions that led to the problem—as opposed to hunting for solutions.
We often mistakenly think that good problem-solving means solving the problem, that is, applying countermeasures, and we may even propose and apply several countermeasures in the hope that one of them will stop the problem. ***In contrast, in Toyota’s way of thinking if the solution to a problem is not yet obvious, it means we have not yet understood the situation sufficiently. Time to go and see ******again***
An example: A factory that makes precision-cast turbine blades for aircraft engines was experiencing a quality problem. One of the last processes in the turbine-blade value stream is a spray-coating line, much like a paint line, and some blades were coming out of the coating process with dents from banging against one another. Due to the damage, these expensive parts would have to be scrapped. Engineers quickly put forth a number of potential countermeasures, such as hanging the blades farther apart on the coating line chain conveyor, putting a protective shield between each blade, and so on.
One engineer took a different approach and simply observed the coating process in action. After about three hours of watching, he noticed something at a point in the process where the chain conveyor makes a 90-degree turn. As the turbine blades went around this corner, some of them would rotate counterclockwise a little and slightly unscrew the hook upon which they were hanging. When the hook became unscrewed far enough, it allowed the blade to swing and on occasion contact the neighboring blade. Once the engineer understood the problem, then the right countermeasure became obvious: prevent the hooks from unscrewing.
***Few of us take the time to keep observing a process until the cause of a problem becomes clear.*** We tend instead to reward firefighters and expediters who temporarily fix a problem…..
## It Keeps Going
***Once you begin working with the improvement kata at a process, there is no end. ***If the target condition is achieved with some consistency day in and day out, it may be time to develop the next target condition for this process. Without a target condition (challenge) to strive for, a process will tend to slip back.
***This is the time to make an overall reflection, to summarize what was learned in this complete improvement kata cycle in preparation for the next.*** While you are working to achieve the current target condition, you will usually begin to see elements of the next target condition (challenge). If not, then you’re probably not struggling enough with process details.
You may not arrive at a target condition 100 percent. For example, it is unlikely that a production process can ever be 100 percent stable. At production processes you may reach a state where you are just reacting to deviations and abnormalities, rather than still striving to reach a challenging target condition. A question I sometimes ask myself is: “Are we still working under a challenge here?” If not, then it may be time to define the next target condition.
***Occasionally you will not achieve a target condition on time, but this is sometimes acceptable. Why? Because we learn the most from failures. ***
For a few years I chaired a manufacturing conference in Munich, and one year several speakers, in presenting improvements they had made, ended their presentations with a photograph of the award—a trophy or plaque—they had won. After this happened a few times in a row, I felt compelled to point out that sure, Toyota too would show its awards, but this would not be the last slide in its presentation. Toyota’s last slide would describe the next challenge***. It is okay to celebrate successes, but we should always be looking ahead and focusing on a target condition and the next step. If we decide to use awards, then they should not be seen as an end, but rather as a beginning, a doorway to more learning. ***
***The benchmark to beat is yourself and your current condition.***
***….***
## Adaptive Persistence
***By embedding the improvement ******kata****** into daily work, Toyota has done something elegant: it has developed a practical and universal method for evolving along unforeseeable routes toward only generally defined long-term visions. This could be called “Adaptive Persistence,” a fitting phrase coined by Richard T. Pascale in his famous 1984 California Management Review article. ***
To paraphrase Mr. Pascale, Toyota’s continued success is not due to perfect up front decisions and plans (that is, perfect aim). Many priorities become clear only as you strive to move toward something, rather than through advance planning. Thousands of PDCA cycles toward target conditions contribute incrementally and cumulatively to Toyota’s cost, quality, and market position. Toyota finds the path along the way based on what is being learned along the way. In hindsight, then, what seems to be strategy emerges.
***Toyota does not really have any solutions to offer us, but rather a means for us to sense situations and develop appropriate, smart responses.*** Toyota’s executives, managers, and leaders are operating on the basis that organization survival arises from adaptation to unfolding events, on the way to a desired condition. They do not think of good versus bad situations, but of problems as something to be expected and as opportunities to more deeply understand and further develop our work processes. Toyota’s strategy for moving toward a vision is target conditions + PDCA; which is to say, the improvement kata. Furthermore, Toyota’s executives, managers, and leaders see as perhaps their main task teaching people the improvement kata in a learn-by-doing mode, which will be the subject of Part IV.
## A Way of Thinking and Acting
***It is important to realize that the improvement kata is about behavior routines. It is a routine of thinking and acting that harnesses our human capability to improve and to solve problems. When we view and interpret what Toyota is doing in this light, it becomes easier to grasp, and we can go further in our own efforts to compete on a similar basis.***
***….***
## Coaching Is Required
Suppose we would like to teach a team of athletes some new skills and have them do well in competition. In this case, we would certainly not expect that simply explaining a different way of doing something, no matter how well that explaining is done, will be sufficient for altering their behavior. It is not possible to objectively assess one’s own performance and see what skills you need to work on. This is because we tend not to perceive our own habits and do not know what we do not know. In the case of athletes, we naturally expect them to practice under the observation and guidance of an experienced coach, and that the necessity of having a coach will not go away. If no one observes them and provides feedback, they can end up internalizing the wrong routines.
It is the same at Toyota, where the improvement kata does not happen automatically or autonomously. Toyota’s managers and leaders work hard every day to both teach it and to keep improvement going in an effective manner. Since improvement and adaptation are central to Toyota’s business philosophy (normal daily management = process improvement), it is not surprising that managers and leaders there work to ensure that improvement happens. What is surprising, however, is how they go about it.
### The Role of Leaders at Toyota
The primary task of Toyota’s managers and leaders does not revolve around improvement per se, but around increasing the improvement capability of people. That capability is what, in Toyota’s view, strengthens the company. Toyota’s managers and leaders develop people who in turn improve processes through the improvement kata.
Developing the improvement capability of people at Toyota is not relegated to the human resources or the training and development departments. It is part of every day’s work in every area, and it is managers and supervisors who are expected to teach their people the improvement kata. The improvement kata is part of how people are managed day to day. This means, of course, that the managers and leaders must themselves be experienced in using the improvement kata.
Because the improvement kata is a set of behavioral guidelines, it is something that we learn through repeated practice. It takes conditioning to make behavioral routines become second nature, and consequently a lot of Toyota’s managerial activities involve having people practice the improvement kata with their guidance. For team leaders and group leaders, this teaching occupies more than 50 percent of their time, and for higher-level managers it can also occupy up to 50 percent. Developing people in this manner is part of a manager’s or supervisor’s evaluation, bonus, promotion, and salary.
### Training While Working On the Real Thing
***We may think of taking classes and seminars to learn, but Toyota thinks of working through actual improvement challenges as how one learns.
